Joas took all the gold and silver, and all the vessels he found in the house of the Lord and in the king's treasuries. He also took hostages, and then he returned to Samaria, the capital of Israel.

(2 Kings 14:15) 
Now, the rest of the acts of Joas, and his military strength, and how he fought against Amasias, the King of Judah — are these not all written in the book of the chronicles of the Kings of Israel?

(2 Kings 14:16) 
Joas then slept with his fathers — meaning he died — and was buried in Samaria with the other Kings of Israel. And his son, Jeroboam, reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 14:17) 
Amasias, the son of Joas and King of Judah, lived for fifteen years after Joas, the son of Joachaz and King of Israel, had died.

(2 Kings 14:18) 
Now, the rest of the acts of Amasias — are these not written in the book of the chronicles of the Kings of Judah?

(2 Kings 14:19) 
A conspiracy was made against Amasias in Jerusalem. He fled to Lachish to escape, but he was killed there.

(2 Kings 14:20) 
His body was then brought back on horses, and he was buried in Jerusalem with his fathers, in the City of David.

(2 Kings 14:21) 
Then all the people of Judah took Azarias, who was sixteen years old, and they appointed him king in place of his father Amasias.

(2 Kings 14:22) 
It was Azarias who rebuilt Ailath and restored it to Judah, after the king — his father — had died.

(2 Kings 14:23) 
In the fifteenth year of Amasias, the son of Joas and King of Judah, Jeroboam, the son of Joas and King of Israel, began to reign in Samaria, and he reigned for forty-one years.

(2 Kings 14:24) 
Jeroboam did what was evil in the sight of the Lord. He did not turn away from any of the sins of Jeroboam, the son of Nabat, who had caused Israel to sin and worship idols.

(2 Kings 14:25) 
It was Jeroboam who restored the borders of Israel, from the entrance of Hamath to the Sea of the Arabah, according to the word of the Lord, the God of Israel. This word was spoken through His servant Jonas, the son of Amathi, the prophet, who was from Gath-hepher.

(2 Kings 14:26) 
For the Lord saw that the affliction of Israel was exceedingly bitter. He saw that they were utterly consumed and trapped, even those who remained, and there was no helper left for Israel.

(2 Kings 14:27) 
The Lord had not yet declared that He would blot out the name of Israel from under the sky — meaning from existence. Instead, He saved them through the hand of Jeroboam, the son of Joas.

(2 Kings 14:28) 
Now, the rest of the acts of Jeroboam, and everything he did, including his military strength, and how he fought, and how he recovered Damascus and Hamath for Judah within Israel — are these not written in the book of the chronicles of the Kings of Israel?

(2 Kings 14:29) 
Jeroboam then slept with his fathers — meaning he died — with the other Kings of Israel. And his son, Zacharias, reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 14:30) 
In the twenty-seventh year of Jeroboam, the King of Israel, Azarias, the son of Amasias and King of Judah, began his reign.

(2 Kings 14:31) 
He was sixteen years old when he began to reign, and he reigned for fifty-two years in Jerusalem. The name of his mother was Jechelia, who was from Jerusalem.

(2 Kings 14:32) 
Azarias did what was pleasing in the sight of the Lord, following all the ways that his father Amasias had done.

(2 Kings 14:33) 
Yet, he did not remove the high places of idolatry; the people still offered sacrifices and burned incense on those high places dedicated to false gods.

(2 Kings 14:34) 
But the Lord struck the king, and he became a leper until the day of his death. He lived in a separate house, isolated because of his illness. Joatham, the king's son, managed the palace and governed the people of the land in his father's stead.

(2 Kings 14:35) 
Now, the rest of the acts of Azarias, and everything he did — are these not written in the book of the chronicles of the Kings of Judah?

(2 Kings 14:36) 
Azarias then slept with his fathers — meaning he died — and they buried him with his fathers in the City of David. And his son, Joatham, reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 14:37) 
In the second year of Pekah, the son of Remaliah and King of Israel, Joatham, the son of Ozias and King of Judah, began his reign.

(2 Kings 14:38) 
He was twenty-five years old when he began to reign, and he reigned for sixteen years in Jerusalem. The name of his mother was Jerusa, the daughter of Sadoc.

(2 Kings 14:39) 
Joatham did what was pleasing in the sight of the Lord, following all the ways that his father Ozias had done.

(2 Kings 14:40) 
Yet, he did not remove the high places of idolatry; the people still offered sacrifices and burned incense on those high places dedicated to false gods. However, he himself built the upper gate of the house of the Lord.

(2 Kings 14:1) 
In the second year of the reign of Joash, the son of Jehoahaz, who was king of Israel, Amaziah, the son of Joash, king of Judah, began his reign.

(2 Kings 14:2) 
He was twenty-five years old when he began to reign, and he reigned for twenty-nine years in Jerusalem. His mother's name was Jehoaddan, from Jerusalem.

(2 Kings 14:3) 
He did what was right in the sight of the Lord God. However, he did not do so perfectly, like his ancestor David. Instead, he did everything just as Joash, his father, had done.

(2 Kings 14:4) 
Nevertheless, Amaziah did not remove the high places, which were unauthorized altars for worship. The people continued to sacrifice and burn incense in these high places.

(2 Kings 14:5) 
After he had secured his royal power, he executed his own servants who had assassinated King Joash, his father.

(2 Kings 14:6) 
But he did not put their children to death. He acted according to what is written in the book of the Law of Moses, as the Lord God had commanded, saying: "Fathers shall not be put to death for the sins of their children, nor shall children be put to death for the sins of their fathers. Rather, each person shall die for their own sin."

(2 Kings 14:7) 
It was Amaziah himself who struck down ten thousand Edomites in the Valley of Salt. He also captured the city of Sela in battle, and he named it Joktheel, a name it bears even to this present day.

(2 Kings 14:8) 
Then Amaziah sent messengers to Joash, the son of Jehoahaz, who was the son of Jehu, king of Israel, saying: "Come, let us confront each other in battle."

(2 Kings 14:9) 
King Joash of Israel sent back a message to King Amaziah of Judah, saying: "The insignificant thistle of Lebanon sent a message to the mighty cedar of Lebanon, saying: 'Give your daughter as a wife to my son.' But then, wild beasts of the field came by and trampled the thistle. This is a warning that you are beneath me."

(2 Kings 14:10) 
You have indeed struck down Edom and gained a victory, and your heart has become proud because of it. Be content with this glory, and stay in your own house. Why provoke such a disaster, that you should fall, and Judah with you?

(2 Kings 14:11) 
But Amaziah would not listen to this warning. So King Joash of Israel went up against him. Both he and King Amaziah of Judah confronted each other at Beth Shemesh, a city in Judah.

(2 Kings 14:12) 
And Judah was defeated before Israel. Every man then fled to his own tent, abandoning the battle.

(2 Kings 14:13) 
King Joash of Israel captured King Amaziah of Judah, the son of Joash, who was the son of Ahaziah, at Beth Shemesh. Joash then brought Amaziah back to Jerusalem and tore down the wall of Jerusalem, from the Gate of Ephraim to the Corner Gate, a distance of four hundred cubits.

(2 Kings 14:14) 
He also took all the gold and silver, and all the vessels that were found in the Temple of the Lord God, as well as in the king's treasuries. He also took hostages, and then returned to Samaria, the capital of his kingdom.

(2 Kings 14:15) 
Now, the rest of the acts of Joash, and his military might, and how he fought against Amaziah, king of Judah—are these not written in the Book of the Chronicles of the Kings of Israel?

(2 Kings 14:16) 
So Joash passed away and was buried in Samaria with the kings of Israel. And Jeroboam, his son, began to reign in his place.

(2 Kings 14:17) 
Amaziah, the son of Joash, king of Judah, lived for fifteen years after the death of Joash, the son of Jehoahaz, king of Israel.

(2 Kings 14:18) 
Now, the rest of the acts of Amaziah—are these not written in the Book of the Chronicles of the Kings of Judah?

(2 Kings 14:19) 
After a conspiracy was formed against him in Jerusalem, Amaziah fled to Lachish. But they sent men after him to Lachish and killed him there.

(2 Kings 14:20) 
They brought his body back on horses, and he was buried in Jerusalem with his fathers, in the City of David.

(2 Kings 14:21) 
Then all the people of Judah took Azariah, who was sixteen years old, and made him king in place of his father Amaziah. (He is also known as Uzziah.)

(2 Kings 14:22) 
It was Azariah who rebuilt Elath and restored it to Judah, after King Amaziah passed away and was buried with his ancestors.

(2 Kings 14:23) 
In the fifteenth year of Amaziah, the son of Joash, who was king of Judah, Jeroboam, the son of Joash, king of Israel, began his reign in Samaria. He reigned for forty-one years.

(2 Kings 14:24) 
And he did what was evil in the sight of the Lord God. He did not turn away from any of the sins of Jeroboam, the son of Nebat, who had led Israel into sin by establishing idol worship.

(2 Kings 14:25) 
It was Jeroboam who restored the borders of Israel, from the entrance of Hamath to the Sea of the Arabah (which is the Dead Sea). He did this according to the word of the Lord God of Israel, which He had spoken through His servant Jonah, the prophet, the son of Amittai, who was from Gath Hepher.

(2 Kings 14:26) 
For the Lord God saw the very bitter affliction of Israel. There was no one left, whether enslaved or free, and no one to help Israel.

(2 Kings 14:27) 
And the Lord God had not said that He would blot out the name of Israel from under heaven. So He saved them by the hand of Jeroboam, the son of Joash.

(2 Kings 14:28) 
Now, the rest of the acts of Jeroboam, and all that he did, and his military might, and how he fought, and how he restored Damascus and Hamath to Judah within Israel—are these not written in the Book of the Chronicles of the Kings of Israel?

(2 Kings 14:29) 
So Jeroboam passed away and was buried with the kings of Israel. And Zechariah, his son, began to reign in his place.

(2 Kings 14:30) 
In the twenty-seventh year of Jeroboam, king of Israel, Azariah, the son of Amaziah, king of Judah, began his reign.

(2 Kings 14:31) 
He was sixteen years old when he began to reign, and he reigned for fifty-two years in Jerusalem. His mother's name was Jecoliah, from Jerusalem.

(2 Kings 14:32) 
And he did what was pleasing in the sight of the Lord God, just as his father Amaziah had done in every way.

(2 Kings 14:33) 
Nevertheless, he did not remove the high places, which were unauthorized altars for worship. The people still continued to offer sacrifices and burn incense there.

(2 Kings 14:34) 
But the Lord God struck the king, and he remained a leper until the day of his death. Because of his leprosy, he had to live in a separate house, isolated from the people. And Jotham, the king's son, was in charge of the royal household and governed the people of the land.

(2 Kings 14:35) 
Now, the rest of the acts of Azariah, and all that he did—are these not written in the Book of the Chronicles of the Kings of Judah?

(2 Kings 14:36) 
So Azariah passed away and was buried with his fathers in the City of David. And Jotham, his son, began to reign in his place.

(2 Kings 14:37) 
In the thirty-eighth year of Azariah, king of Judah, Zechariah, the son of Jeroboam, reigned over Israel in Samaria for six months.

(2 Kings 14:38) 
And he did what was evil in the sight of the Lord God, just as his fathers had done. He did not turn away from the sins of Jeroboam, the son of Nebat, who had led Israel into sin.

(2 Kings 14:39) 
And Shallum, the son of Jabesh, conspired against him. Shallum struck him down in public, killed him, and then began to reign in his place.

(2 Kings 14:40) 
Now, the rest of the acts of Zechariah—are these not written in the Book of the Chronicles of the Kings of Israel?

(2 Kings 14:1) 
In the second year of Joas, who was the son of Joachaz and king of Israel, Amasias, the son of Joas, began his reign as king over Judah.

(2 Kings 14:2) 
He was twenty-five years old when he began to reign, and he reigned for twenty-nine years in Jerusalem. His mother's name was Joadan, and she was from Jerusalem.

(2 Kings 14:3) 
He did what was right in the sight of the Lord, yet not like David, his great ancestor. He did everything just as his father Joas had done.

(2 Kings 14:4) 
However, he did not remove the high places of worship. The people still continued to sacrifice and burn incense there.

(2 Kings 14:5) 
When he had taken firm control of his kingdom, he executed the servants who had murdered Joas, his royal father.

(2 Kings 14:6) 
But he did not put the children of those murderers to death, according to what is written in the book of the law of Moses—for the Lord had commanded this, saying: "Fathers shall not die for their children, nor shall children die for their fathers, but each one shall die for his own sin."

(2 Kings 14:7) 
He himself struck down Edom in the Valley of Salt, ten thousand men; and he captured the city of Sela in battle, and he called its name Jectehel, which it is still called to this very day.

(2 Kings 14:8) 
Then Amasias sent messengers to Joas, the son of Joachaz and grandson of Jehu, king of Israel, saying: "Come, let us look each other in the face"—meaning, "Let us fight in battle."

(2 Kings 14:9) 
Joas, the king of Israel, sent back a reply to Amasias, king of Judah, saying: "The thistles of the field sent word to the cedars of Lebanon, saying: 'Give your daughter to my son as a wife.' But then, wild beasts of the field came by and trampled down the thistles."

(2 Kings 14:10) 
"You have indeed struck down Edom and you have grown arrogant because of it. Be content with your victory and remain in your own house. Why provoke such evil against yourself, that both you and Judah should fall with you?"

(2 Kings 14:11) 
But Amasias would not listen to the warning. So Joas, the king of Israel, went up against him, and Amasias, the king of Judah, also went forth; and they faced each other in Bethsames, a town in Judah.

(2 Kings 14:12) 
Judah was defeated by Israel in the battle, and every man of Judah fled back to his own tent.

(2 Kings 14:13) 
Joas, the king of Israel, captured Amasias, the king of Judah (who was the son of Joas, and grandson of Ochozias), at Bethsames, and brought him to Jerusalem. Then Joas tore down the wall of Jerusalem, from the Gate of Ephraim to the Corner Gate, a length of four hundred cubits—which is about 600 feet.

(2 Kings 14:14) 
He also took all the gold and silver, and all the vessels that he found in the house of the Lord and in the king's treasuries, and he brought the hostages back with him to Samaria.

(2 Kings 14:15) 
Now, the rest of the acts of Joas, and his strength, and how he fought against Amasias, the king of Judah—are these not written in the book of the words of the days of the kings of Israel?

(2 Kings 14:16) 
So Joas slept with his fathers, and he was buried in Samaria with the kings of Israel; and Jeroboam, his son, reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 14:17) 
Amasias, the son of Joas, king of Judah, lived fifteen years after the death of Joas, the son of Joachaz, king of Israel.

(2 Kings 14:18) 
Now, the rest of the acts of Amasias—are these not written in the book of the words of the days of the kings of Judah?

(2 Kings 14:19) 
And a conspiracy was formed against him in Jerusalem, so he fled to Lachis. But they sent men after him to Lachis, and they put him to death there.

(2 Kings 14:20) 
And they brought his body back on horses, and he was buried in Jerusalem with his fathers, in the City of David.

(2 Kings 14:21) 
Then all the people of Judah took Azarias, who was sixteen years old, and they made him king in place of his father Amasias.

(2 Kings 14:22) 
It was he who built Ailath and restored it to Judah, after the king—his father—had slept with his fathers.

(2 Kings 14:23) 
In the fifteenth year of Amasias, the son of Joas and king of Judah, Jeroboam, the son of Joas, began to reign as king over Israel in Samaria, and he reigned for forty-one years.

(2 Kings 14:24) 
And he did what was evil in the sight of the Lord. He did not turn away from any of the sins of Jeroboam, the son of Nabat, who had made Israel to sin.

(2 Kings 14:25) 
It was he who restored the borders of Israel, from the entrance of Emath as far as the Sea of the Desert, according to the word of the Lord, the God of Israel, which He spoke through His servant Jonas, the son of Amathi, the prophet, who was from Geth-opher.

(2 Kings 14:26) 
For the Lord saw that the affliction of Israel was exceedingly bitter, and that they were utterly consumed and shut in, and there was no helper for Israel.

(2 Kings 14:27) 
And the Lord had not said that He would blot out the name of Israel from under the heavens; rather, He saved them by the hand of Jeroboam, the son of Joas.

(2 Kings 14:28) 
Now, the rest of the acts of Jeroboam, and all that he did, and his strength, how he fought, and how he restored Damascus and Emath to Judah in Israel—are these not written in the book of the words of the days of the kings of Israel?

(2 Kings 14:29) 
So Jeroboam slept with his fathers, the kings of Israel, and Zacharias, his son, reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 14:1) 
In the second year that Joas, the son of Joachaz, was king of Israel, Amasias, the son of Joas, began his reign as king of Judah.

(2 Kings 14:2) 
He was twenty-five years old when he began to reign, and he reigned for twenty-nine years in Jerusalem. His mother's name was Joadan, and she was from Jerusalem.

(2 Kings 14:3) 
He did what was right in the sight of the Lord; however, not in the same manner as his ancestor David. Amasias did everything that was to be done according to the ways of Joas his father.

(2 Kings 14:4) 
Nevertheless, he did not remove the high places of worship. The people continued to sacrifice and burn incense there.

(2 Kings 14:5) 
As soon as he had firmly established his kingship, he executed the servants who had murdered his father, the king.

(2 Kings 14:6) 
However, he did not put to death the children of the murderers. He acted according to what is written in the Book of the Law of Moses, for the Lord had commanded, saying: 'Fathers shall not be put to death for their children, nor shall children be put to death for their fathers; rather, each person shall die for their own sin.'

(2 Kings 14:7) 
Amasias himself struck down ten thousand Edomites in the Valley of Salt, and he captured Petra—the city then called Sela—in battle. He then renamed it Jectheel, which it is still called to this very day.

(2 Kings 14:8) 
Then Amasias sent messengers to Joas, the son of Joachaz, the son of Jehu, king of Israel, saying: 'Come, let us meet face-to-face in battle!'

(2 Kings 14:9) 
Joas, king of Israel, sent back this reply to Amasias, king of Judah: 'A lowly lily of the field once sent a message to the mighty cedar of Lebanon, saying: "Give your daughter to my son as a wife." But then, wild beasts of the field came by and trampled the lily underfoot.'

(2 Kings 14:10) 
You have struck down Edom, and your heart has become arrogant because of it. Be content with your glory, and remain in your own palace! Why do you provoke this evil that will cause both you and Judah to fall with you?'

(2 Kings 14:11) 
But Amasias would not yield. So Joas, king of Israel, went up with his forces, and he and Amasias, king of Judah, met face-to-face in Beth-shemesh of Judah for battle.

(2 Kings 14:12) 
Judah was defeated by Israel, and every man fled home to his own dwelling.

(2 Kings 14:13) 
Joas, king of Israel, captured Amasias, king of Judah—the son of Joas and grandson of Ochozias—in Beth-shemesh. He then brought Amasias to Jerusalem and demolished a section of Jerusalem's city wall, from the Gate of Ephraim to the Corner Gate, a distance of four hundred cubits.

(2 Kings 14:14) 
Joas took all the gold and silver, along with all the vessels he found in the Temple of the Lord and in the royal treasuries. He also took hostages, and then he returned with all of it to Samaria.

(2 Kings 14:15) 
As for the rest of the deeds of Joas, and all his might, and how he fought against Amasias, king of Judah—are these not all written in the Book of the Chronicles of the Kings of Israel?

(2 Kings 14:16) 
Joas died and was buried in Samaria alongside the other kings of Israel. His son, Jeroboam, then reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 14:17) 
Amasias, the son of Joas, king of Judah, lived for fifteen years after the death of Joas, the son of Joachaz, king of Israel.

(2 Kings 14:18) 
As for the rest of the deeds of Amasias, are these not all written in the Book of the Chronicles of the Kings of Judah?

(2 Kings 14:19) 
When a conspiracy was formed against him in Jerusalem, Amasias fled to Lachish. But his enemies sent men after him to Lachish and murdered him there.

(2 Kings 14:20) 
His body was brought back on horses, and he was buried in Jerusalem with his ancestors in the City of David.

(2 Kings 14:21) 
Then all the people of Judah took Azarias, who was sixteen years old, and made him king in place of his father Amasias.

(2 Kings 14:22) 
It was Azarias who rebuilt Ailath and restored it to Judah, after his father, the king, had died.

(2 Kings 14:23) 
In the fifteenth year of the reign of Amasias, the son of Joas, king of Judah, Jeroboam, the son of Joas, began his reign as king of Israel in Samaria, and he reigned for forty-one years.

(2 Kings 14:24) 
He did what was evil in the sight of the Lord. He did not turn away from any of the sins of Jeroboam, the son of Nabat, who had caused Israel to sin.

(2 Kings 14:25) 
It was Jeroboam who restored the borders of Israel, from the entrance of Hamath all the way to the Sea of the Arabah—the Dead Sea—just as the Lord, the God of Israel, had declared. This was spoken through His servant Jonas, the prophet, the son of Amathi, who was from Gath-hepher.

(2 Kings 14:26) 
For the Lord had seen how very bitter was the affliction of Israel. They were utterly consumed, and there was no one left to help, neither bond nor free; no one to come to Israel's aid.

(2 Kings 14:27) 
The Lord had not yet declared that He would blot out the name of Israel from the face of the earth. So, He saved them by the hand of Jeroboam, the son of Joas.

(2 Kings 14:28) 
As for the rest of the deeds of Jeroboam, and all that he did, and his might—how he fought his battles, and how he restored Damascus and Hamath, previously held by Judah, back to Israel—are these not all written in the Book of the Chronicles of the Kings of Israel?

(2 Kings 14:29) 
Jeroboam died and was buried in Samaria with the kings of Israel. Then his son Zacharias reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 15:1) 
In the twenty-seventh year of King Jeroboam's reign in Israel, Azariah, the son of King Amaziah of Judah, began his reign.

(2 Kings 15:2) 
He was sixteen years old when he became king, and he reigned for fifty-two years in Jerusalem. His mother's name was Jechelia, who was from Jerusalem.

(2 Kings 15:3) 
He did what was right in the sight of the Lord, just as his father Amaziah had done in all things.

(2 Kings 15:4) 
Nevertheless, he did not remove the high places of worship. The people still continued to sacrifice and burn incense on these high places.

(2 Kings 15:5) 
Therefore, the Lord struck the king with leprosy, and he remained a leper until the day of his death. He lived in a private house, set apart from others. Jotham, the king's son, managed the royal household and governed the people of the land.

(2 Kings 15:6) 
All the other deeds of Azariah, and everything he accomplished, are they not written in the book of the chronicles of the kings of Judah?

(2 Kings 15:7) 
Azariah died and was buried with his ancestors in the City of David. His son Jotham then reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 15:8) 
In the thirty-eighth year of Azariah's reign in Judah, Zechariah, the son of Jeroboam, reigned over Israel in Samaria for six months.

(2 Kings 15:9) 
He did what was evil in the sight of the Lord, just as his ancestors had done. He did not turn away from the sins of Jeroboam, son of Nebat, who had led Israel into sin.

(2 Kings 15:10) 
Shallum, son of Jabesh, conspired against him. He struck Zechariah down in public, killing him, and then reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 15:11) 
All the other deeds of Zechariah, are they not written in the book of the chronicles of the kings of Israel?

(2 Kings 15:12) 
This fulfilled the word of the Lord which He had spoken to Jehu, saying, "Your sons shall sit on the throne of Israel for four generations." And so it came to pass.

(2 Kings 15:13) 
Shallum, son of Jabesh, began to reign in the thirty-ninth year of Uzziah, king of Judah. He reigned for only one month in Samaria.

(2 Kings 15:14) 
Then Menahem, son of Gadi, went up from Tirzah to Samaria. He struck down Shallum, son of Jabesh, in Samaria and killed him, then he reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 15:15) 
All the other deeds of Shallum, and the conspiracy he carried out, are they not written in the book of the chronicles of the kings of Israel?

(2 Kings 15:16) 
At that time, Menahem struck down Tappuah and everyone in it, and all its territory, starting from Tirzah, because they had not opened their gates to him. He killed all the pregnant women there and ripped them open.

(2 Kings 15:17) 
In the thirty-ninth year of Azariah's reign in Judah, Menahem, son of Gadi, began his ten-year reign over Israel in Samaria.

(2 Kings 15:18) 
He did what was evil in the sight of the Lord. He never departed from the sins of Jeroboam, son of Nebat, who had led Israel into sin, throughout all his days.

(2 Kings 15:19) 
Pul, the king of the Assyrians, came to the land. Menahem gave Pul a thousand talents of silver so that Pul would help him and strengthen his reign.

(2 Kings 15:20) 
Menahem exacted the money from the people of Israel, from all the powerful and wealthy men, demanding fifty shekels of silver from each, to give to the king of the Assyrians. So the king of Assyria turned back and did not remain in the land.

(2 Kings 15:21) 
All the other deeds of Menahem, and everything he accomplished, are they not written in the book of the chronicles of the kings of Israel?

(2 Kings 15:22) 
Menahem died and was buried with his ancestors. His son Pekahiah then reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 15:23) 
In the fiftieth year of Azariah's reign in Judah, Pekahiah, son of Menahem, reigned over Israel in Samaria for two years.

(2 Kings 15:24) 
He did what was evil in the sight of the Lord. He did not turn away from the sins of Jeroboam, son of Nebat, who had led Israel into sin.

(2 Kings 15:25) 
However, Pekah, son of Remaliah, his captain, conspired against him. He had fifty men from the sons of the Gileadites with him. He struck Pekahiah down in Samaria, in the citadel of the royal palace, near Argob and Arieh. He killed him and then reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 15:26) 
All the other deeds of Pekahiah, and everything he accomplished, are they not written in the book of the chronicles of the kings of Israel?

(2 Kings 15:27) 
In the fifty-second year of Azariah's reign in Judah, Pekah, son of Remaliah, reigned over Israel in Samaria for twenty years.

(2 Kings 15:28) 
He did what was evil in the sight of the Lord. He did not turn away from the sins of Jeroboam, son of Nebat, who had led Israel into sin.

(2 Kings 15:29) 
In the days of Pekah, king of Israel, Tiglath-Pileser, king of the Assyrians, came and captured Ijon, Abel-Beth-Maachah, Janoah, Kedesh, Hazor, Gilead, Galilee, and all the land of Naphtali. He deported the inhabitants of these places to Assyria.

(2 Kings 15:30) 
Then Hoshea, son of Elah, conspired against Pekah, son of Remaliah, striking him down and killing him. He then reigned in his place, in the twentieth year of Jotham, son of Uzziah.

(2 Kings 15:31) 
All the other deeds of Pekah, and everything he accomplished, are they not written in the book of the chronicles of the kings of Israel?

(2 Kings 15:32) 
In the second year of Pekah's reign, son of Remaliah, king of Israel, Jotham, son of Uzziah, king of Judah, began his reign.

(2 Kings 15:33) 
He was twenty-five years old when he became king, and he reigned for sixteen years in Jerusalem. His mother's name was Jerusha, daughter of Zadok.

(2 Kings 15:34) 
He did what was right in the sight of the Lord, just as his father Uzziah had done in all things.

(2 Kings 15:35) 
Nevertheless, he did not remove the high places of worship. The people still continued to sacrifice and burn incense on these high places. He himself built the upper gate of the house of the Lord.

(2 Kings 15:36) 
All the other deeds of Jotham, and everything he accomplished, are they not written in the book of the chronicles of the kings of Judah?

(2 Kings 15:37) 
In those days, the Lord began to send Rezin, king of Syria, and Pekah, son of Remaliah, against Judah.

(2 Kings 15:38) 
Jotham died and was buried with his ancestors in the City of David, his father's city. His son Ahaz then reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 15:1) 
In the twenty-seventh year of King Jeroboam's reign over Israel, Azariah, the son of King Amaziah of Judah, began to reign as king of Judah. —This Jeroboam was Jeroboam II, the son of Joash.

(2 Kings 15:2) 
He was fifteen years old when he began to reign, and he reigned for fifty-two years in Jerusalem. His mother's name was Jechelia, from Jerusalem.

(2 Kings 15:3) 
And he did what was right in the sight of the Lord, just as his father Amaziah had done in all things.

(2 Kings 15:4) 
Nevertheless, he did not remove the high places; for the people still offered sacrifices and burned incense there. —These 'high places' were unauthorized sites of worship outside of the Temple in Jerusalem.

(2 Kings 15:5) 
But the Lord struck the king with leprosy, and he remained a leper until the day of his death. He lived in a separate house, while Jotham, the king's son, governed the royal household and judged the people of the land.

(2 Kings 15:6) 
Now the rest of the acts of Azariah, and all that he did, are they not written in the Book of the Chronicles of the Kings of Judah?

(2 Kings 15:7) 
So Azariah slept with his fathers, and they buried him with his fathers in the City of David. His son Jotham then reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 15:8) 
In the thirty-eighth year of Azariah's reign over Judah, Zachariah, the son of Jeroboam (Jeroboam II), began to reign over Israel for six months.

(2 Kings 15:9) 
And he did what was evil in the sight of the Lord, just as his royal ancestors had done. He did not turn away from the sins introduced by Jeroboam, the son of Nabat, who had led Israel into idolatry and false worship. —The 'sins of Jeroboam' refer to the worship of golden calves at Dan and Bethel.

(2 Kings 15:10) 
But Shallum, the son of Jabesh, conspired against him. He struck him down openly, killing him, and then reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 15:11) 
Now the rest of the acts of Zachariah, are they not written in the Book of the Chronicles of the Kings of Israel?

(2 Kings 15:12) 
This fulfilled the word of the Lord which He had spoken to Jehu, saying: 'Your sons shall sit on the throne of Israel until the fourth generation.' And so it came to pass. —This prophecy was fulfilled with Zachariah, the fourth descendant of Jehu.

(2 Kings 15:13) 
Shallum, the son of Jabesh, began to reign in the thirty-ninth year of King Azariah of Judah. He reigned for only one month in Samaria.

(2 Kings 15:14) 
Then Menahem, the son of Gadi, went up from Tirzah to Samaria. He struck down Shallum, the son of Jabesh, in Samaria, killed him, and then reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 15:15) 
Now the rest of the acts of Shallum, and the conspiracy by which he conspired, are they not written in the Book of the Chronicles of the Kings of Israel?

(2 Kings 15:16) 
Then Menahem struck down Tiphsah, and all who were in it, and its territories from Tirzah, because they had not opened their gates to him. He killed all the pregnant women, ripping them open.

(2 Kings 15:17) 
In the thirty-ninth year of Azariah's reign over Judah, Menahem, the son of Gadi, began to reign over Israel for ten years in Samaria.

(2 Kings 15:18) 
And he did what was evil in the sight of the Lord. He did not turn away from the sins introduced by Jeroboam, the son of Nabat, who had caused Israel to sin, throughout all his days.

(2 Kings 15:19) 
Pul, the king of the Assyrians, came into the land of Israel. Menahem, in an act of submission, gave Pul a thousand talents of silver so that Pul would offer him military aid and strengthen his hold on the kingdom. —Pul is also known as Tiglath-Pileser III, a powerful Assyrian king.

(2 Kings 15:20) 
Menahem then commanded all the wealthy men of Israel to give money to Pul, each man contributing five hundred shekels of silver, to secure the help of the king of Assyria. So the king of Assyria returned to his own land and did not remain in Israel.

(2 Kings 15:21) 
Now the rest of the acts of Menahem, and all that he did, are they not written in the Book of the Chronicles of the Kings of Israel?

(2 Kings 15:22) 
So Menahem slept with his fathers. His son Pekahiah then reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 15:23) 
In the fiftieth year of Azariah's reign over Judah, Pekahiah, the son of Menahem, began to reign over Israel in Samaria for two years.

(2 Kings 15:24) 
And he did what was evil in the sight of the Lord. He did not turn away from the sins introduced by Jeroboam, the son of Nabat, who had caused Israel to sin.

(2 Kings 15:25) 
But Pekah, the son of Remaliah, his own captain, conspired against him. Pekah struck him down in Samaria, in the citadel of the royal palace, in the presence of Argob and Arie, along with fifty men from the Gileadites. Pekah killed him and then reigned in his place. —The 'citadel of the royal palace' refers to a fortified inner part of the king's residence.

(2 Kings 15:26) 
Now the rest of the acts of Pekahiah, and all that he did, are they not written in the Book of the Chronicles of the Kings of Israel?

(2 Kings 15:27) 
In the fifty-second year of Azariah's reign over Judah, Pekah, the son of Remaliah, began to reign over Israel in Samaria for twenty years.

(2 Kings 15:28) 
And he did what was evil in the sight of the Lord. He did not turn away from the sins introduced by Jeroboam, the son of Nabat, who had caused Israel to sin.

(2 Kings 15:29) 
In the days of Pekah, king of Israel, Tiglath-Pileser, the king of Assyria, came and captured Ijon, Abel-Beth-Maachah, Janoah, Kedesh, Hazor, Gilead, Galilee, and all the land of Naphtali. He then deported their inhabitants to Assyria. —This Tiglath-Pileser is the same King Pul mentioned earlier, and this deportation was a common Assyrian tactic to suppress rebellion.

(2 Kings 15:30) 
Then Hoshea, the son of Elah, conspired against Pekah, the son of Remaliah. He struck him down and killed him, and then reigned in his place in the twentieth year of Jotham, the son of Azariah.

(2 Kings 15:31) 
Now the rest of the acts of Pekah, and all that he did, are they not written in the Book of the Chronicles of the Kings of Israel?

(2 Kings 15:32) 
In the second year of Pekah's reign over Israel (Pekah, the son of Remaliah), Jotham, the son of King Azariah of Judah, began to reign as king of Judah.

(2 Kings 15:33) 
He was twenty-five years old when he began to reign, and he reigned for sixteen years in Jerusalem. His mother's name was Jerusha, the daughter of Zadok.

(2 Kings 15:34) 
And he did what was right in the sight of the Lord, just as his father Azariah had done in all things.

(2 Kings 15:35) 
Nevertheless, he did not remove the high places; for the people still offered sacrifices and burned incense there. It was he who built the Upper Gate of the House of the Lord. —The 'high places' were unauthorized sites of worship outside of the Temple in Jerusalem.

(2 Kings 15:36) 
Now the rest of the acts of Jotham, and all that he did, are they not written in the Book of the Chronicles of the Kings of Judah?

(2 Kings 15:37) 
In those days, the Lord began to send against Judah King Rezin of Syria, and Pekah, the son of Remaliah. —Pekah was the reigning king of Israel at this time.

(2 Kings 15:38) 
So Jotham slept with his fathers, and he was buried with them in the City of David, his father. His son Ahaz then reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 16:1) 
In the seventeenth year of the reign of Pekah, son of Remaliah, Ahaz, son of Jotham, who was the King of Judah, began his reign.

(2 Kings 16:2) 
Ahaz was twenty years old when he began to reign, and he reigned for sixteen years in Jerusalem. He did not do what was pleasing in the sight of the Lord his God, unlike his father David.

(2 Kings 16:3) 
Instead, he walked in the way of the kings of Israel. Furthermore, he even made his son pass through fire, following the abominable customs of the nations whom the Lord had driven out before the children of Israel.

(2 Kings 16:4) 
He also sacrificed and burned incense in the high places, and upon the hills, and under every leafy tree, engaging in idolatry.

(2 Kings 16:5) 
Then Rezin, the king of Syria, and Pekah, son of Remaliah, the king of Israel, came up to Jerusalem to wage war. Although they besieged Ahaz, they could not capture him.

(2 Kings 16:6) 
At that time, Rezin, the king of Syria, restored Elath to Syria, driving the Judeans out of Elath. And the Edomites came into Elath and settled there, where they remain even to this very day.

(2 Kings 16:7) 
Then Ahaz sent messengers to Tiglath-Pileser, the king of the Assyrians, saying: "I am your servant and your son. Come up and rescue me from the hand of the king of Syria and from the hand of the king of Israel, who have risen up against me."

(2 Kings 16:8) 
Ahaz then took the silver and gold that could be found in the house of the Lord and in the royal treasuries, and he sent these as gifts to the king of the Assyrians.

(2 Kings 16:9) 
The Assyrian king agreed to his request, for the king of the Assyrians went up to Damascus, devastated it, and deported its inhabitants to Cyrene. He also killed Rezin.

(2 Kings 16:10) 
And King Ahaz went to Damascus to meet Tiglath-Pileser, the king of the Assyrians. When King Ahaz saw the altar in Damascus, he sent its detailed plan and the exact likeness of all its workmanship to Urijah, the priest.

(2 Kings 16:11) 
And Urijah, the priest, built an altar exactly according to all the instructions King Ahaz had sent from Damascus. Urijah the priest did this, completing it before King Ahaz returned from Damascus.

(2 Kings 16:12) 
When the king returned from Damascus, he saw the altar, and he began to offer sacrifices and vows upon it.

(2 Kings 16:13) 
He burned his burnt offering and his sacrifice, poured out his libations, and sprinkled the blood of his peace offerings upon the altar.

(2 Kings 16:14) 
Furthermore, the bronze altar that stood before the Lord, he moved it away from the front of the Temple, from its place between the altar and the Lord's Temple, and placed it on the side of this new, foreign altar.

(2 Kings 16:15) 
King Ahaz also commanded Urijah the priest, saying: "Upon the larger, new altar, offer the morning burnt offering, the evening sacrifice, the king's burnt offering, and his sacrifice, along with the burnt offering of all the people of the land, their sacrifices, and their libations. You shall pour all the blood of the burnt offerings and all the sacrifices upon it. As for the bronze altar, it shall be available for my personal discretion and use."

(2 Kings 16:16) 
So Urijah, the priest, did according to all that King Ahaz had commanded.

(2 Kings 16:17) 
King Ahaz also cut off the panels of the stands and removed the basins from them. He also took the great bronze Sea off the twelve oxen-shaped bases that were beneath it, removing it from its stands, and placed it upon a stone pavement.

(2 Kings 16:18) 
The covered passageway for the Sabbath, which he had built in the Temple, and the outer entrance for the king, he removed these from the Temple of the Lord, doing so out of deference to the king of the Assyrians.

(2 Kings 16:19) 
Now, the rest of the deeds of Ahaz, all that he did, are these not written in the book of the chronicles of the kings of Judah?

(2 Kings 16:20) 
Ahaz slept with his fathers and was buried with them in the City of David. And Hezekiah, his son, reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 16:1) 
In the seventeenth year of Pekah, the son of Remaliah, King Ahaz, the son of Jotham, began to reign over Judah.

(2 Kings 16:2) 
Ahaz was twenty years old when he became king. He reigned for sixteen years in Jerusalem. He did not do what was right in the sight of the Lord his God, unlike his father David, who served God faithfully.

(2 Kings 16:3) 
Instead, he followed the ways of the kings of Israel (who were unfaithful to God). He even sacrificed his own son by fire, according to the abominable practices of the pagan nations that the Lord had previously driven out from before the Israelites.

(2 Kings 16:4) 
He also offered sacrifices and burned incense on the high places (illicit altars), on the hills, and under every flourishing tree, worshipping pagan deities in these places.

(2 Kings 16:5) 
At that time, King Rezin of Syria and Pekah, the son of Remaliah, King of Israel, marched up to Jerusalem to wage war. Although they besieged Ahaz, they were not able to overcome him.

(2 Kings 16:6) 
At that same time, King Rezin of Syria restored Elath (also known as Eilat or Ailath) to Syria, driving the Jews out of Elath. The Edomites then came into Elath and settled there, where they remained even until the present day.

(2 Kings 16:7) 
King Ahaz then sent messengers to King Tiglath-Pileser of Assyria, declaring: "I am your servant and your son (meaning, I pledge loyalty and vassalage to you). Come up and rescue me from the power of the King of Syria and from the power of the King of Israel, who have risen up against me."

(2 Kings 16:8) 
Ahaz then took all the silver and gold that could be found in the Temple of the Lord and in the royal treasuries, and he sent these valuables as tribute (or gifts) to the King of Assyria.

(2 Kings 16:9) 
The King of Assyria agreed to Ahaz's request. He marched up to Damascus, conquered and devastated it, and deported its inhabitants to Kir (or Cyrene). He also put King Rezin to death.

(2 Kings 16:10) 
King Ahaz then traveled to Damascus to meet King Tiglath-Pileser of Assyria. While there, Ahaz saw the altar in Damascus (which was likely a pagan, Assyrian-style altar). King Ahaz then sent a detailed sketch of this altar, including its full design and every aspect of its construction, to Uriah the priest in Jerusalem.

(2 Kings 16:11) 
So Uriah the priest diligently built an altar exactly according to all the specifications and instructions that King Ahaz had sent him from Damascus. Uriah the priest completed this task before King Ahaz even returned from Damascus.

(2 Kings 16:12) 
When the king returned from Damascus, he saw the new altar. He approached it and venerated it (showing reverence to this foreign, pagan-inspired structure). He then went up to it and offered sacrifices upon it.

(2 Kings 16:13) 
He burned his burnt offerings and his other sacrifices on it, and poured out his drink offerings. He also sprinkled the blood of his peace offerings upon this altar.

(2 Kings 16:14) 
Furthermore, Ahaz removed the bronze altar that stood before the Lord (the sacred altar originally built by Solomon), which was in front of the Temple, specifically between the new altar and the Temple of the Lord. He moved it from its proper place in front of the Lord’s Temple and positioned it to the side of the new altar.

(2 Kings 16:15) 
King Ahaz then commanded Uriah the priest, saying: "On this larger, new altar (the one copied from Damascus), you are to offer the morning burnt offering and the evening sacrifice. Also offer the king's burnt offering and his sacrifice, along with the burnt offerings of all the people of the land, their sacrifices, and their drink offerings. All the blood from the burnt offerings and all the blood from the sacrifices you shall pour out upon it. The bronze altar (the old, sacred one) will now be reserved for my personal consultation and discretion."

(2 Kings 16:16) 
So Uriah the priest carried out all these instructions, exactly as King Ahaz had commanded him.

(2 Kings 16:17) 
King Ahaz also removed the bronze stands and the basins that rested upon them. He took down the "Sea" (the large bronze basin for ritual washing) from the bronze oxen that supported it, and he placed the "Sea" upon a paved stone platform instead.

(2 Kings 16:18) 
He also removed the covered way for the Sabbath (or "Sabbath structure") that had been built in the Temple. Furthermore, he rerouted the king's outer entrance to the Temple of the Lord, making these changes specifically to appease the King of Assyria and avoid any offense or suspicion.

(2 Kings 16:19) 
Now, the rest of the acts of Ahaz, all that he did, are these not written in the Book of the Chronicles of the Kings of Judah?

(2 Kings 16:20) 
Ahaz rested with his ancestors and was buried with them in the City of David. His son Hezekiah then reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 17:1) 
In the twelfth year of King Ahaz of Judah, Hoshea, son of Elah, began to reign over Israel in Samaria, and he reigned for nine years.

(2 Kings 17:2) 
But he did what was evil in the sight of the Lord, though not as evil as the kings of Israel who had reigned before him.

(2 Kings 17:3) 
Shalmaneser, the king of the Assyrians, marched against King Hoshea, and Hoshea became his servant and paid tribute to him.

(2 Kings 17:4) 
But the king of Assyria discovered that Hoshea was conspiring against him. Hoshea had sent messengers to the king of Egypt and had stopped paying his yearly tribute to Assyria. So the king of Assyria arrested Hoshea, chained him, and imprisoned him.

(2 Kings 17:5) 
Then the king of Assyria invaded all of the land of Israel. He marched to Samaria and besieged it for three years.

(2 Kings 17:6) 
In the ninth year of Hoshea’s reign, the king of Assyria captured Samaria and carried the people of Israel into exile to Assyria. He settled them in Halah, and on the Habor River in Gozan, and in the cities of the Medes.

(2 Kings 17:7) 
This disaster happened because the people of Israel had sinned against the Lord their God, who had brought them up out of the land of Egypt and freed them from the power of Pharaoh, the king of Egypt. Yet they worshipped other gods.

(2 Kings 17:8) 
They followed the practices of the pagan nations that the Lord had driven out before them. They also imitated the practices of the kings of Israel, which were just as evil.

(2 Kings 17:9) 
The people of Israel secretly did things against the Lord their God that were not right. They built high places for idol worship in all their cities, from watchtowers to fortified cities.

(2 Kings 17:10) 
They set up sacred stones and Asherah poles—idolatrous symbols—on every high hill and under every green tree.

(2 Kings 17:11) 
On all these high places, they burned incense, just like the pagan nations that the Lord had driven out before them. They did wicked things that provoked the Lord’s anger.

(2 Kings 17:12) 
They worshipped idols, even though the Lord had told them, “You must not do such a thing.”

(2 Kings 17:13) 
The Lord had warned both Israel and Judah through all his prophets and seers, saying, “Turn from your evil ways. Keep My commandments and My ceremonies according to all the Law that I commanded your fathers and that I sent to you through My servants, the prophets.”

(2 Kings 17:14) 
But they would not listen. They became stubborn, just like their fathers, who had refused to obey the Lord their God.

(2 Kings 17:15) 
They rejected His decrees and the covenant He had made with their fathers, as well as the warnings He had given them. They pursued worthless idols and themselves became worthless. They imitated the pagan nations around them, even though the Lord had commanded them not to do as those nations did.

(2 Kings 17:16) 
They abandoned all the commandments of the Lord their God. They made for themselves two calves of cast metal, and Asherah poles. They worshipped all the host of the universe—the stars and planets—and they served Baal.

(2 Kings 17:17) 
They sacrificed their sons and daughters in fire as offerings to idols. They practiced divination and omens, and they surrendered themselves to doing evil in the sight of the Lord, provoking His anger.

(2 Kings 17:18) 
So the Lord was very angry with Israel and removed them from His sight. Only the tribe of Judah was left.

(2 Kings 17:19) 
Even Judah did not keep the commandments of the Lord their God; instead, they followed the sinful practices that Israel had introduced.

(2 Kings 17:20) 
Therefore, the Lord rejected all the people of Israel. He afflicted them and delivered them into the hands of plunderers until He finally cast them out from His presence.

(2 Kings 17:21) 
For Israel had been torn away from the house of David, and they made Jeroboam, son of Nebat, their king. Jeroboam then led Israel away from the Lord and caused them to commit a great sin.

(2 Kings 17:22) 
The people of Israel continued in all the sins that Jeroboam had committed, and they did not turn away from them.

(2 Kings 17:23) 
So the Lord removed Israel from His presence, just as He had spoken through all His servants, the prophets. Thus, Israel was carried away from their own land into Assyria, where they remain to this very day.

(2 Kings 17:24) 
Then the king of Assyria brought people from Babylon, Cuthah, Avva, Hamath, and Sepharvaim, and settled them in the cities of Samaria in place of the people of Israel. These new inhabitants took possession of Samaria and lived in its cities.

(2 Kings 17:25) 
When these new inhabitants first began to live there, they did not worship the Lord, so the Lord sent lions among them, which killed some of them.

(2 Kings 17:26) 
So the king of Assyria was informed, “The nations whom you have deported and settled in the cities of Samaria do not know the customs of the God of that land. Therefore, the Lord has sent lions among them, and they are killing them because they do not know the proper way to worship the God of that land.”

(2 Kings 17:27) 
Then the king of Assyria gave this command: “Send back one of the priests whom you captured from there. Let him go and live among them and teach them the customs of the God of that land.”

(2 Kings 17:28) 
So one of the priests who had been carried away captive from Samaria came and lived in Bethel, and he taught them how they should worship the Lord.

(2 Kings 17:29) 
Nevertheless, each of these foreign nations made their own gods and placed them in the shrines on the high places that the Samaritans had built. Each nation did this in the cities where they lived.

(2 Kings 17:30) 
The people from Babylon made Succoth-Benoth; the people from Cuthah made Nergal; and the people from Hamath made Ashima.

(2 Kings 17:31) 
The Avvites made Nibhaz and Tartak. And the people from Sepharvaim burned their children in fire as offerings to Adrammelech and Anammelech, the gods of Sepharvaim.

(2 Kings 17:32) 
They did worship the Lord, but they also appointed priests from among themselves for the high places, and these priests offered sacrifices in the shrines of the high places.

(2 Kings 17:33) 
So they worshipped the Lord, but they also served their own gods according to the customs of the nations from which they had been brought.

(2 Kings 17:34) 
Even to this present day, they continue to practice their former customs. They do not truly fear the Lord, nor do they observe His ceremonies, His judgments, His Law, or the commandment that the Lord gave to the children of Jacob, to whom He gave the name Israel.

(2 Kings 17:35) 
When the Lord had made a covenant with them, He commanded them, saying, “You must not fear other gods, nor worship them, nor serve them, nor sacrifice to them.

(2 Kings 17:36) 
But you must fear the Lord your God, who brought you up out of the land of Egypt with great power and an outstretched arm. Him alone you must fear, Him alone you must worship, and to Him alone you must sacrifice.

(2 Kings 17:37) 
You must always be careful to observe the ceremonies, the judgments, the Law, and the commandments that He wrote for you. You must not fear other gods.

(2 Kings 17:38) 
And the covenant that I made with you, you must not forget. You must not worship other gods.

(2 Kings 17:39) 
Instead, fear the Lord your God, and He Himself will deliver you from the power of all your enemies.”

(2 Kings 17:40) 
However, they did not listen, but continued to do according to their former customs.

(2 Kings 17:1) 
In the twelfth year of King Achaz of Judah, Osee, the son of Ela, began his reign over Israel in Samaria, and he ruled for nine years.

(2 Kings 17:2) 
And Osee committed evil acts in the presence of the Lord, though not to the same degree as the previous kings of Israel before him.

(2 Kings 17:3) 
Salmanasar, the king of the Assyrians, marched against Osee, and Osee became Salmanasar's vassal and paid him annual tribute.

(2 Kings 17:4) 
But the king of the Assyrians discovered that Osee was secretly conspiring against him, sending messengers to King Sua of Egypt. Osee also stopped paying the tribute he used to send every year. So the king of Assyria besieged Osee and imprisoned him.

(2 Kings 17:5) 
The king of Assyria then marched throughout all the land of Israel, and when he reached Samaria, he besieged it for three years.

(2 Kings 17:6) 
In the ninth year of Osee's reign, the king of Assyria captured Samaria and carried the people of Israel away as exiles to Assyria. He settled them in Hala and in Habor, near the Gozan River, and in the cities of the Medes.

(2 Kings 17:7) 
This happened because the children of Israel had sinned against the Lord their God, the One who had brought them out of the land of Egypt and delivered them from the power of Pharaoh, the king of Egypt. Yet they worshipped other gods.

(2 Kings 17:8) 
They also followed the customs of the pagan nations whom the Lord had driven out before the children of Israel, and the customs that the kings of Israel had themselves established.

(2 Kings 17:9) 
The children of Israel secretly committed acts against the Lord their God that were not right. They built high places for themselves in all their cities, from the watchman's tower to the fortified city.

(2 Kings 17:10) 
They set up sacred pillars and wooden idols for themselves on every high hill and under every green tree.

(2 Kings 17:11) 
There, on those altars, they burned incense in the manner of the pagan nations whom the Lord had removed from before them. They committed very wicked deeds, provoking the Lord to anger.

(2 Kings 17:12) 
They worshipped filthy idols, concerning which the Lord had explicitly commanded them not to engage in such practices.

(2 Kings 17:13) 
The Lord had warned Israel and Judah through all His prophets and seers, saying: "Turn away from your wicked ways, and keep My commandments and My ceremonies, according to all the Law that I commanded your fathers, and as I sent to you through the hand of My servants, the prophets."

(2 Kings 17:14) 
But they would not listen; instead, they stiffened their necks, just like their fathers had, who refused to obey the Lord their God.

(2 Kings 17:15) 
They rejected His statutes, and the covenant He had made with their fathers, and the warnings He had given them. They followed worthless idols and themselves became worthless. They imitated the pagan nations around them, concerning whom the Lord had commanded them not to act like them.

(2 Kings 17:16) 
They abandoned all the commandments of the Lord their God. They cast for themselves two calves out of metal, and made wooden idols, and worshipped all the heavenly bodies—the entire host of the universe—and served Baal.

(2 Kings 17:17) 
They sacrificed their sons and daughters in fire, and devoted themselves to divination and omens. They committed much evil in the sight of the Lord, provoking Him to anger.

(2 Kings 17:18) 
The Lord became intensely angry with Israel and removed them from His sight. Only the tribe of Judah remained.

(2 Kings 17:19) 
Yet even Judah did not keep the commandments of the Lord their God. Instead, they followed the superstitious practices that Israel had established.

(2 Kings 17:20) 
So the Lord rejected all the descendants of Israel, and afflicted them, and gave them into the hand of plunderers until He finally cast them out from His presence.

(2 Kings 17:21) 
From the time that Israel split from the house of David and appointed Jeroboam, the son of Nabat, as their king, Jeroboam led Israel away from the Lord and caused them to commit a great sin.

(2 Kings 17:22) 
The children of Israel continued in all the sins that Jeroboam had committed, and they did not turn away from them.

(2 Kings 17:23) 
Until the Lord finally removed Israel from His sight, just as He had spoken through all His servants, the prophets. Thus, Israel was carried away from their own land into Assyria, where they remain to this very day.

(2 Kings 17:24) 
The king of Assyria brought people from Babylon, Cutha, Avah, Emath, and Sepharvaim and settled them in the cities of Samaria in place of the children of Israel. These new inhabitants took possession of Samaria and lived in its cities.

(2 Kings 17:25) 
When they first settled there, these people did not fear the Lord. So the Lord sent lions among them, which killed some of them.

(2 Kings 17:26) 
Then it was reported to the king of Assyria, saying: "The nations whom you have relocated and settled in the cities of Samaria do not know the laws of the God of that land. Therefore, the Lord has sent lions among them, and indeed, they are killing them, because these people do not know the proper way to worship the God of that land."

(2 Kings 17:27) 
So the king of Assyria commanded, saying: "Send back one of the priests whom you brought as captives from there. Let him go and live among them and teach them the laws of the God of the land."

(2 Kings 17:28) 
So one of the priests who had been carried away captive from Samaria came and settled in Bethel. He taught them how they should worship the Lord, the true God.

(2 Kings 17:29) 
Yet, each of the new nations made their own gods. They placed these gods in the shrines of the high places which the Samaritans had originally built, each nation in the cities where they lived.

(2 Kings 17:30) 
For the men from Babylon made Succoth-benoth. The men from Cutha made Nergal. The men from Hamath made Ashima.

(2 Kings 17:31) 
The Avvites made Nibhaz and Tartak. And the people from Sepharvaim burned their children as offerings in fire to Adrammelech and Anammelech, the gods of Sepharvaim.

(2 Kings 17:32) 
Even though they were worshipping the Lord, they also appointed priests for their high places from among themselves—these were not true priests of God—and these false priests served in the shrines of the high places.

(2 Kings 17:33) 
While they worshipped the Lord, they also served their own gods, according to the custom of the nations from which they had been brought to Samaria.

(2 Kings 17:34) 
To this very day, they continue to practice their former customs. They do not truly fear the Lord, nor do they keep His ceremonies, His judgments, His law, or the commandment which the Lord had given to the children of Jacob, whom He had named Israel.

(2 Kings 17:35) 
The Lord had made a covenant with them and commanded them, saying: "You shall not fear other gods, nor worship them, nor serve them, nor sacrifice to them."

(2 Kings 17:36) 
But the Lord, who brought you up out of the land of Egypt with great power and an outstretched arm, Him you shall fear, Him you shall worship, and to Him alone you shall sacrifice.

(2 Kings 17:37) 
And the ceremonies, the judgments, the law, and the commandments which He wrote for you, you shall diligently keep and perform forever. And you shall not fear other gods.

(2 Kings 17:38) 
And the covenant that I made with you, do not forget it. You shall not worship other gods.

(2 Kings 17:39) 
But you shall fear the Lord your God, and He Himself will deliver you from the hand of all your enemies.

(2 Kings 17:40) 
Yet these people did not listen to the Lord's commands. Instead, they continued to act according to their former, unholy customs.

(2 Kings 17:1) 
In the twelfth year that Achaz was king of Judah, Osee, the son of Ela, began to reign over Israel in Samaria. He ruled for nine years.

(2 Kings 17:2) 
And Osee did what was evil in the sight of the Lord. His wickedness was not like the kings of Israel who had been before him, but still evil.

(2 Kings 17:3) 
Salmanasar, the king of the Assyrians, marched against Osee. Osee became his servant and had to pay tribute to him.

(2 Kings 17:4) 
But the king of the Assyrians discovered that Osee was conspiring for help. He had sent messengers to King Sua of Egypt and had stopped paying the annual tribute he used to pay. So, the king of Assyria besieged Osee and put him in prison.

(2 Kings 17:5) 
The Assyrian king then went up and laid waste to all the land of Israel, and he came to Samaria, the capital city, and besieged it for three years.

(2 Kings 17:6) 
In the ninth year of Osee’s reign, the king of the Assyrians captured Samaria. He then deported the people of Israel to Assyria, settling them in Hala, and in Habor by the Gozan River, and in the cities of the Medes. This was the end of the Northern Kingdom.

(2 Kings 17:7) 
This all happened because the children of Israel had sinned against the Lord their God. He was the God who had delivered them from the land of Egypt, from the tyranny of Pharaoh, the king of Egypt. But they chose to worship foreign gods instead.

(2 Kings 17:8) 
They had walked according to the rituals of the pagan nations whom the Lord had destroyed before the children of Israel, and according to the customs that their own kings of Israel had established. Because of this, the Israelites themselves had behaved in exactly the same way as those pagans.

(2 Kings 17:9) 
The children of Israel had also spoken things that were not right against the Lord their God, and they had built high places for idol worship in all their cities—everywhere from the watchtowers of shepherds to the fortified cities.

(2 Kings 17:10) 
They had set up pillars and sacred groves for idol worship on every high hill and under every shady tree, even though God had forbidden these practices.

(2 Kings 17:11) 
There, on these altars, they burned incense just like the pagan nations whom the Lord had driven out from before them. They committed the worst abominations, constantly provoking the Lord to anger.

(2 Kings 17:12) 
They worshipped all the defiling idols and did everything that the Lord had specifically commanded them not to do.

(2 Kings 17:13) 
The Lord had solemnly warned both Israel and Judah through all His prophets and seers, saying: "Turn back from your evil ways and keep My commandments and My ceremonies, according to all the Law that I commanded your fathers. Do as I sent to you through the hands of My servants, the prophets."

(2 Kings 17:14) 
But they refused to listen. Instead, they stiffened their necks, just like their fathers had, those who had refused to obey the Lord their God.

(2 Kings 17:15) 
They rejected His statutes and the covenant He had made with their fathers, and they ignored the warnings by which He had testified to them. They pursued empty idols and behaved in empty ways, following the pagan nations around them, concerning whom the Lord had explicitly commanded them not to act like those nations.

(2 Kings 17:16) 
They abandoned all the commandments of the Lord their God. They made two molten calves for themselves, and they worshipped Asherah poles, and bowed down to all the host of heaven—the stars and planets which were worshipped as gods. They also served Baal.

(2 Kings 17:17) 
They made their sons and their daughters pass through fire as offerings to false gods. They practiced divination and used omens, giving themselves over to doing evil in the sight of the Lord, constantly provoking Him to anger.

(2 Kings 17:18) 
So the Lord became extremely angry with Israel and removed them from His sight. Only the tribe of Judah was left.

(2 Kings 17:19) 
Yet even Judah itself did not keep the commandments of the Lord its God. Indeed, they walked in the same errors that Israel had committed.

(2 Kings 17:20) 
The Lord then rejected all the offspring of Israel, afflicting them and delivering them into the hands of plunderers until He finally cast them out from His presence entirely.

(2 Kings 17:21) 
For Israel had indeed been torn away from the house of David. They made Jeroboam, the son of Nabat, their king. He then turned Israel away from the Lord and caused them to commit a great sin.

(2 Kings 17:22) 
And the children of Israel continued to walk in all the sins that Jeroboam had committed. They never departed from these sins,

(2 Kings 17:23) 
until the Lord finally removed Israel from His sight, just as He had spoken through all His servants, the prophets. So, Israel was carried away from their own land into Assyria, and they remain there to this very day.

(2 Kings 17:24) 
Then the king of Assyria brought people from Babylon, from Cutha, from Ava, from Emath, and from Sepharvaim. He settled them in the cities of Samaria in place of the children of Israel. These new inhabitants took possession of Samaria and lived in its cities.

(2 Kings 17:25) 
When they first began to live there, they did not fear the Lord. So the Lord sent lions among them, which killed some of them.

(2 Kings 17:26) 
So it was reported to the king of Assyria, saying: "The nations whom you have deported and settled in the cities of Samaria do not know the statutes of the God of the land. Because of this, the Lord has sent lions among them, and behold, they are killing them, because these people do not know the custom of the God of the land."

(2 Kings 17:27) 
The king of Assyria then commanded, saying: "Bring back one of the priests whom you took captive from there. Let him go and live with them, and teach them the statutes of the God of the land."

(2 Kings 17:28) 
So one of the priests who had been taken captive from Samaria came and lived in Bethel. He taught them how they should worship the Lord.

(2 Kings 17:29) 
But each nation still made its own gods in each of their cities. They put these idols in the shrines of the high places which the Samaritans had built, each nation doing so in the cities where they lived.

(2 Kings 17:30) 
For example, the men from Babylon made Succoth Benoth, the men from Cutha made Nergal, and the men from Emath made Ashima.

(2 Kings 17:31) 
Furthermore, the Avvites made Nibaz and Tartak. Those from Sepharvaim even burned their children in fire as offerings to Adrammelech and Anammelech, the gods of Sepharvaim.

(2 Kings 17:32) 
Nevertheless, they also worshipped the Lord. They appointed priests for their high places from among their own people, and these priests served in the shrines of the high places.

(2 Kings 17:33) 
So, while they worshipped the Lord, they also served their own gods according to the custom of the nations from which they had been brought. This syncretism was an abomination to God.

(2 Kings 17:34) 
To this very day, they continue to do things according to their old customs. They do not truly fear the Lord, nor do they observe His ceremonies, or His judgments, or His Law, or the commandment that the Lord had given to the children of Jacob, whom He had named Israel.

(2 Kings 17:35) 
For God had made a covenant with the Israelites and had commanded them, saying: "You shall not worship foreign gods, nor bow down to them, nor serve them, nor offer sacrifices to them. You must worship only Me."

(2 Kings 17:36) 
But you shall worship the Lord, who brought you out of the land of Egypt with great power and with an outstretched arm. You shall worship Him alone and offer sacrifices to Him alone.

(2 Kings 17:37) 
And you shall carefully observe the ceremonies, the judgments, the Law, and the commandment which He wrote for you, so that you may do them at all times. You shall not fear foreign gods.

(2 Kings 17:38) 
And you shall not forget the covenant that I made with you, nor shall you worship foreign gods.

(2 Kings 17:39) 
Instead, you shall worship the Lord your God, and He Himself will deliver you from the hand of all your enemies.

(2 Kings 17:40) 
But they did not listen. Instead, they continued to do things according to their old, sinful customs.

(2 Kings 18:1) 
In the third year that Osee, the son of Ela, was king over Israel, Ezechias, the son of Achaz, began to reign as king over Juda.

(2 Kings 18:2) 
He was twenty-five years old when he started his reign, and he reigned for twenty-nine years in Jerusalem. The name of his mother was Abi, daughter of Zacharias.

(2 Kings 18:3) 
And he did what was good and pleasing before the Lord, just as his ancestor David had done in all things.

(2 Kings 18:4) 
He himself tore down the high places of idolatry, broke apart the pagan statues, cut down the Asherah poles, and crushed the bronze serpent that Moses had made. This was because, up until that time, the sons of Israel had been burning incense to it, and he called it Nohestan, meaning "a piece of bronze."

(2 Kings 18:5) 
He placed his hope and trust in the Lord, the God of Israel. There was no king like him among all the kings of Juda after him, nor among those who came before him.

(2 Kings 18:6) 
And he held fast to the Lord, not departing from His ways, and he kept His commandments, which He had commanded to Moses.

(2 Kings 18:7) 
For this reason, the Lord was with him. And in all that he undertook, he acted prosperously. He also rebelled against the king of the Assyrians and no longer served him.

(2 Kings 18:8) 
He himself struck down the Philistines all the way to Gaza, conquering all their borders, from the watchtower of the shepherds to the fortified city.

(2 Kings 18:9) 
In the fourth year of King Ezechias' reign, which was the seventh year that Osee, the son of Ela, was king of Israel, Salmanasar, the king of the Assyrians, went up against Samaria and besieged it.

(2 Kings 18:10) 
And he captured it after three years, in the sixth year of Ezechias' reign, which was the ninth year that Osee was king of Israel. That is when Samaria was taken.

(2 Kings 18:11) 
And Salmanasar exiled the people of Israel to Assyria. He settled them in Hala and by the Habor River, which is in Gozan, and in the cities of the Medes.

(2 Kings 18:12) 
This happened because they had not listened to the voice of the Lord their God but had violated His covenant. They did not listen to or obey all the commands that Moses, the servant of the Lord, had given them.

(2 Kings 18:13) 
In the fourteenth year of King Ezechias' reign, Sennacherib, the king of the Assyrians, marched against all the fortified cities of Juda and captured them.

(2 Kings 18:14) 
Then Ezechias, the king of Juda, sent messengers to the king of the Assyrians in Lachis, saying, "I have sinned; withdraw from me, and I will bear whatever you impose upon me." So the king of the Assyrians demanded from Ezechias, the king of Juda, three hundred talents of silver and thirty talents of gold.

(2 Kings 18:15) 
And Ezechias gave him all the silver that could be found in the house of the Lord and in the royal treasuries.

(2 Kings 18:16) 
At that time, Ezechias broke off the doors of the temple of the Lord and the gold plates that he himself had affixed to them, and he gave them to the king of the Assyrians.

(2 Kings 18:17) 
But the king of the Assyrians sent Tharthan, Rabsaris, and Rabsaces from Lachis to King Ezechias with a great army to Jerusalem. When they arrived, they came to Jerusalem and stood by the aqueduct of the Upper Pool, which is on the road of the fuller's field.

(2 Kings 18:18) 
And they called out for the king. But Eliacim, the son of Helcias, who was in charge of the household, along with Sobna the scribe and Joahe, the son of Asaph, the recorder, went out to them instead.

(2 Kings 18:19) 
And Rabsaces said to them, "Speak to Ezechias: 'Thus says the great king, the king of the Assyrians: What is this confidence in which you trust?'"

(2 Kings 18:20) 
"'Perhaps you have devised a plan and gathered strength to wage war. In what do you trust that you dare to rebel?'"

(2 Kings 18:21) 
"'Are you relying on that broken reed of a staff, Egypt, upon which if a man leans, it will shatter and pierce his hand? Such is Pharao, the king of Egypt, to all who trust in him.'"

(2 Kings 18:22) 
"'But if you answer me, "We trust in the Lord our God," is it not He whose high places and altars Ezechias has removed, telling Juda and Jerusalem, "You shall worship before these altars in Jerusalem"?'"

(2 Kings 18:23) 
"'Now therefore, give a pledge to my lord, the king of the Assyrians, and I will give you two thousand horses, if you can provide riders for them yourself.'"

(2 Kings 18:24) 
"'And how can you resist even one of the least of my master's servants? Do you have confidence in Egypt because of their chariots and horsemen?'"

(2 Kings 18:25) 
"'Have I come up to this place to destroy it without the Lord's permission? The Lord Himself said to me, "Go up against this land and destroy it."'

(2 Kings 18:26) 
Then Eliacim, the son of Helcias, and Sobna, and Joahe said to Rabsaces, "We beg you to speak to your servants in Aramaic, because we understand that language. Do not speak to us in the language of Juda while the people on the wall are listening."

(2 Kings 18:27) 
And Rabsaces answered them, "Has my master sent me to your master and to you to speak these words? Was it not rather to the men who sit on the wall, so that they might eat their own excrement and drink their own urine along with you?" This crude statement was meant to provoke fear and disgust among the common people.

(2 Kings 18:28) 
So Rabsaces stood up and cried out with a loud voice in the language of Juda, saying, "Hear the words of the great king, the king of the Assyrians!"

(2 Kings 18:29) 
"Thus says the king: 'Do not let Ezechias deceive you, for he will not be able to deliver you from my hand.'"

(2 Kings 18:30) 
"'Nor let him make you trust in the Lord, saying, "The Lord will surely deliver us, and this city will not be handed over to the king of the Assyrians."'

(2 Kings 18:31) 
"'Do not listen to Ezechias! For thus says the king of the Assyrians: Make peace with me and come out to me. Then each of you will eat from his own vineyard and from his own fig tree, and drink water from his own cisterns.'"

(2 Kings 18:32) 
"'Until I come and take you away to a land that is like your own land—a land of grain and wine, a land of bread and vineyards, a land of olive groves and oil and honey. You will live there and not die. Do not listen to Ezechias, who deceives you by saying, "The Lord will deliver us."'

(2 Kings 18:33) 
"'Have any of the gods of the nations truly delivered their land from the hand of the king of the Assyrians?'"

(2 Kings 18:34) 
"'Where are the gods of Emath and Arphad? Where are the gods of Sepharvaim, Ana, and Ava? Did they deliver Samaria from my hand?'"

(2 Kings 18:35) 
"'Which are the gods among all the lands that have delivered their land from my hand, that the Lord might be able to deliver Jerusalem from my hand?'"

(2 Kings 18:36) 
But the people remained silent and did not answer him a single word, for the king had commanded them, saying, "Do not answer him."

(2 Kings 18:37) 
So Eliacim, the son of Helcias, who was in charge of the household, along with Sobna the scribe and Joahe, the son of Asaph, the recorder, came to Ezechias with their clothes torn, and they reported to him the words of Rabsaces.

(2 Kings 18:1) 
In the third year of King Hosea, the son of Elah, who was the king of the northern kingdom of Israel, Hezekiah, the son of King Ahaz, began his reign over the southern kingdom of Judah.

(2 Kings 18:2) 
Hezekiah was twenty-five years old when he started to reign, and he ruled for twenty-nine years in Jerusalem. His mother's name was Abi, the daughter of Zechariah.

(2 Kings 18:3) 
Hezekiah lived a life that pleased the Lord, just as his ancestor David had done.

(2 Kings 18:4) 
Hezekiah destroyed the pagan high places, shattered the idol statues, cut down the Asherah poles used for idolatry, and broke into pieces the bronze serpent that Moses had made. Even up to that time, the people of Israel had been burning incense to it, worshipping it as an idol. Hezekiah called it Nehushtan, which means 'a piece of bronze'.

(2 Kings 18:5) 
Hezekiah placed his trust in the Lord, the God of Israel. Among all the kings of Judah, there was no one like him, neither before him nor after him.

(2 Kings 18:6) 
He remained faithful to the Lord and did not turn away from following Him. He obeyed the commandments that the Lord had given to Moses.

(2 Kings 18:7) 
Because of this, the Lord was with him, and Hezekiah prospered in everything he undertook. He also rebelled against the king of Assyria and refused to serve him any longer.

(2 Kings 18:8) 
Hezekiah attacked and defeated the Philistines, conquering their territory all the way to Gaza and its borders, from the watchtowers of the small villages to the fortified cities.

(2 Kings 18:9) 
In the fourth year of King Hezekiah’s reign, which was also the seventh year of Hosea, the son of Elah, king of the northern kingdom of Israel, Shalmaneser, the king of Assyria, marched up against Samaria and laid siege to it.

(2 Kings 18:10) 
After three years, Shalmaneser captured Samaria. This happened in the sixth year of Hezekiah, which was the ninth year of King Hosea of the northern kingdom of Israel.

(2 Kings 18:11) 
The Assyrian king then deported the people of Israel to Assyria. He settled them in Halah, and along the Habor River in Gozan, and in the cities of the Medes—a historical event that marked the end of the northern kingdom.

(2 Kings 18:12) 
This happened because they did not listen to the voice of the Lord their God. They had broken His covenant and had not obeyed any of the commandments that Moses, the servant of the Lord, had given them.

(2 Kings 18:13) 
In the fourteenth year of King Hezekiah's reign, Sennacherib, the king of Assyria, marched up and attacked all the fortified cities of Judah and captured them.

(2 Kings 18:14) 
Then King Hezekiah of Judah sent messengers to the king of Assyria at Lachish, saying: "I have sinned. Withdraw your forces from me, and whatever tribute you demand, I will pay." So the king of Assyria imposed upon King Hezekiah of Judah a payment of three hundred talents of silver and thirty talents of gold.

(2 Kings 18:15) 
Hezekiah gave him all the silver that could be found in the house of the Lord and in the royal treasuries.

(2 Kings 18:16) 
At that time, Hezekiah even stripped the gold from the doors of the Lord's Temple and from the doorposts that he himself had overlaid with gold, and he gave it all to the king of Assyria.

(2 Kings 18:17)
