But the king of Assyria sent his chief commander, his chief officer, and his field commander from Lachish with a large army to King Hezekiah in Jerusalem. When they had marched up, they arrived at Jerusalem and took up a position by the aqueduct of the Upper Pool, which is on the road to the Washerman's Field.

(2 Kings 18:18) 
They called for the king. So Eliakim, the son of Hilkiah, who was in charge of the palace, Shebna the secretary, and Joah, the son of Asaph, the recorder, went out to meet them.

(2 Kings 18:19) 
Then the field commander spoke to them, saying: "Tell Hezekiah: 'This is what the great king, the king of Assyria, says: What is this confidence in which you trust, that you think can save you?'"

(2 Kings 18:20) 
"You might think you have a strategy or the resources to prepare for war, but tell me, on what do you rely that you dare to defy me?"

(2 Kings 18:21) 
"Are you perhaps relying on that broken reed, Egypt, upon which if a man leans, it splinters and pierces his hand? That is exactly what Pharaoh, the king of Egypt, is to all who trust in him."

(2 Kings 18:22) 
"But if you say to me, 'We trust in the Lord our God,' then I ask: 'Is this not the God whose high places and altars Hezekiah removed? And Hezekiah commanded Judah and Jerusalem to worship only before this one altar in Jerusalem?'"

(2 Kings 18:23) 
"Now, therefore, make a wager with my lord, the king of Assyria. I will give you two thousand horses, if you can find enough riders for them from among your people."

(2 Kings 18:24) 
"How then can you resist even one of my master's least significant officials? You must be relying on Egypt for chariots and horsemen, as you have no strength of your own."

(2 Kings 18:25) 
"Furthermore, do you think I have come up against this place to destroy it without the Lord's will? No, the Lord Himself said to me: 'Go up against this land and destroy it.'"

(2 Kings 18:26) 
Then Eliakim, the son of Hilkiah, and Shebna, and Joah said to the field commander: "Please speak to your servants in Aramaic, for we understand it. Do not speak to us in the language of Judah while the people who are on the wall are listening, as it will demoralize them."

(2 Kings 18:27) 
But the field commander replied to them: "Has my master sent me to speak these words only to your master and to you? Is it not also to the men who are sitting on the wall, who will soon be forced to eat their own dung and drink their own urine along with you, because of the siege?"

(2 Kings 18:28) 
So the field commander stood up and shouted in a loud voice, in the language of Judah, saying: "Hear the words of the great king, the king of Assyria!"

(2 Kings 18:29) 
"This is what the king says: 'Do not let Hezekiah deceive you, for he cannot deliver you from my hand.'"

(2 Kings 18:30) 
"Nor should he persuade you to rely on the Lord, saying: 'The Lord will surely deliver us, and this city will not be handed over to the king of Assyria.'"

(2 Kings 18:31) 
"Do not listen to Hezekiah. For this is what the king of Assyria says: 'Make peace with me and surrender to me. Then each of you will be able to enjoy your own vineyard and fig tree, and drink water from your own cisterns—a promise of prosperity under Assyrian rule.'"

(2 Kings 18:32) 
"Until I come and take you away to a land like your own, a land of grain and new wine, a land of bread and vineyards, a land of olive groves and honey. You will live there and not die. Do not listen to Hezekiah, who deceives you by saying: 'The Lord will deliver us.'"

(2 Kings 18:33) 
"Have the gods of any of the nations ever delivered their land from the hand of the king of Assyria?"

(2 Kings 18:34) 
"Where are the gods of Hamath and Arpad? Where are the gods of Sepharvaim, Hena, and Ivvah? Have they delivered Samaria from my hand?"

(2 Kings 18:35) 
"Which of all the gods of these lands has delivered his land from my hand, that the Lord could deliver Jerusalem from my hand?"

(2 Kings 18:36) 
But the people remained silent and did not answer him a single word, for the king had given a strict command: "Do not answer him."

(2 Kings 18:37) 
Then Eliakim, the son of Hilkiah, who was in charge of the palace, Shebna the secretary, and Joah, the son of Asaph, the recorder, came to Hezekiah with their clothes torn in an expression of grief, and reported to him the words of the field commander.

(2 Kings 18:1) 
It was during the third year that Osee, the son of Ela, ruled as king of Israel, when Ezechias, the son of Achaz, began to reign as king of Judah.

(2 Kings 18:2) 
Ezechias was twenty-five years old when he began his reign, and he ruled in Jerusalem for twenty-nine years. His mother’s name was Abi, the daughter of Zacharias.

(2 Kings 18:3) 
He did what was good and pleasing in the sight of the Lord, just as his father David had done in all things.

(2 Kings 18:4) 
It was Ezechias who destroyed the high places of idolatry, broke apart the pagan statues, cut down the Asherah poles, and crushed the bronze serpent that Moses had made. The reason he did this was because, up to that very time, the children of Israel were still offering incense to it, and he named it Nohestan, meaning a mere piece of bronze.

(2 Kings 18:5) 
Ezechias placed his hope and trust in the Lord, the God of Israel. No king like him, among all the kings of Judah, arose after him, nor among those who ruled before him.

(2 Kings 18:6) 
He remained faithful to the Lord and did not turn away from His commandments. He kept all the commands that the Lord had given to Moses.

(2 Kings 18:7) 
The Lord was with him, and in everything he undertook, he acted with wisdom. He even rebelled against the king of the Assyrians and refused to serve him.

(2 Kings 18:8) 
It was Ezechias who struck down the Philistines, conquering all their territory from the watchtowers of their outposts all the way to the fortified cities, extending his victory even to Gaza.

(2 Kings 18:9) 
In the fourth year of King Ezechias’ reign — which was also the seventh year that Osee, the son of Ela, ruled as king of Israel — Salmanasar, the king of the Assyrians, marched against Samaria and besieged the city.

(2 Kings 18:10) 
And after three years of siege, Salmanasar took the city. This occurred in the sixth year of Ezechias’ reign, which was the ninth year that Osee ruled as king of Israel, when Samaria was finally captured.

(2 Kings 18:11) 
Then the king of Assyria deported the people of Israel into Assyria, settling them in Hala, and in Habor by the river Gozan, and in the cities of the Medes.

(2 Kings 18:12) 
This happened because they did not listen to the voice of the Lord their God. Instead, they broke His covenant and did not obey or follow any of the commandments that Moses, the servant of the Lord, had given them.

(2 Kings 18:13) 
In the fourteenth year of King Ezechias’ reign, Sennacherib, the king of Assyria, marched against all the fortified cities of Judah and captured them.

(2 Kings 18:14) 
At that time, Ezechias, the king of Judah, sent messengers to the king of Assyria in Lachis, saying, "I have sinned. Withdraw from me, and whatever you impose on me, I will bear it." So the king of Assyria demanded from Ezechias, the king of Judah, three hundred talents of silver and thirty talents of gold.

(2 Kings 18:15) 
And Ezechias gave him all the silver that could be found in the House of the Lord and in the royal treasuries.

(2 Kings 18:16) 
At that same time, Ezechias cut off the gold from the doors of the Lord’s temple and from the gold plates which he himself had affixed to them, and he gave them to the king of Assyria.

(2 Kings 18:17) 
Nevertheless, the king of Assyria sent Tharthan, Rabsaris, and Rabsacen from Lachis to King Ezechias with a powerful army against Jerusalem. When they arrived, they came to Jerusalem and took up positions by the aqueduct of the Upper Pool, which is on the road to the Fuller’s Field.

(2 Kings 18:18) 
They called for the king, and so Eliacim, the son of Helcias, who was in charge of the palace, along with Sobna the scribe and Joahe, the son of Asaph, the recorder, went out to meet them.

(2 Kings 18:19) 
Then Rabsacen said to them, "Speak to Ezechias: ‘This is what the great king, the king of Assyria, says: What is this confidence in which you are trusting?’"

(2 Kings 18:20) 
“‘Perhaps you have formed a plan to prepare yourself for war, but in what do you place your trust that you dare to defy me?’"

(2 Kings 18:21) 
“‘Do you truly hope and trust in Egypt, that broken reed staff, which if a man leans on it, will splinter and pierce his hand? That is exactly what Pharaoh, the king of Egypt, is like to all who trust in him.’"

(2 Kings 18:22) 
“‘But if you say to me, "We trust in the Lord our God," is He not the very one whose high places and altars Ezechias removed? Did Ezechias not command Judah and Jerusalem saying, "You shall worship only before this single altar in Jerusalem?"’"

(2 Kings 18:23) 
“‘Now therefore, make a pledge to my lord, the king of Assyria, and I will give you two thousand horses, if you are able to provide riders for them from your own forces.’"

(2 Kings 18:24) 
“‘How then can you resist even one of the least of my master’s servants? Do you truly place your trust in Egypt because of their chariots and horsemen?’"

(2 Kings 18:25) 
“‘Have I come up against this place to destroy it without the Lord’s approval? No! The Lord Himself said to me, "Go up against this land and destroy it."’"

(2 Kings 18:26) 
Then Eliacim, the son of Helcias, and Sobna, and Joahe said to Rabsacen, "We beg you, please speak to your servants in Aramaic, for we understand it. Do not speak to us in the language of Judah while the people who are on the wall are listening."

(2 Kings 18:27) 
But Rabsacen answered them, "Has my master sent me to your master and to you alone to speak these words? Is it not rather to the men who sit on the wall, so that they may be forced to eat their own dung and drink their own urine along with you?"

(2 Kings 18:28) 
So Rabsacen stood and cried out with a loud voice in the language of Judah, saying, "Hear the word of the great king, the king of Assyria!"

(2 Kings 18:29) 
“‘This is what the king says: "Do not let Ezechias deceive you, for he will not be able to deliver you from my hand."’"

(2 Kings 18:30) 
“‘Nor let him persuade you to trust in the Lord by saying, "The Lord will surely deliver us, and this city will not be given into the hand of the king of Assyria."’"

(2 Kings 18:31) 
“‘Do not listen to Ezechias! For this is what the king of Assyria says: "Make peace with me and come out to me. Then each of you will be able to eat from your own vine and your own fig tree, and drink water from your own cisterns.’"

(2 Kings 18:32) 
“‘Until I come and take you away to a land that is like your own land — a land of grain and wine, a land of fruit, olive groves, olive oil, and honey. You will live there and not die. Do not listen to Ezechias, who deceives you by saying, "The Lord will deliver us."’"

(2 Kings 18:33) 
“‘Have the gods of the other nations ever delivered their own land from the hand of the king of Assyria?’"

(2 Kings 18:34) 
“‘Where are the gods of Emath and Arphad? Where are the gods of Sepharvaim, Ana, and Ava? Did they deliver Samaria from my hand?’"

(2 Kings 18:35) 
“‘Which of all the gods of these lands has delivered his land from my hand, that the Lord should be able to deliver Jerusalem from my hand?’"

(2 Kings 18:36) 
But the people remained silent and did not answer him a single word, because the king’s command was clear: "Do not answer him."

(2 Kings 18:37) 
Then Eliacim, the son of Helcias, who was in charge of the palace, and Sobna the scribe, and Joahe, the son of Asaph, the recorder, came to Ezechias with their clothes torn, and they reported to him the words of Rabsacen.

(2 Kings 19:1) 
When King Hezekiah heard this news, he tore his garments to show his great distress, wrapped himself in sackcloth to show his sorrow and repentance, and went into the Temple of the Lord to pray.

(2 Kings 19:2) 
Then King Hezekiah sent Eliakim, the steward of his palace, and Shebna the royal scribe, and the senior priests (all of them clothed in sackcloth, just as the King was, to show their shared sorrow), to the Prophet Isaiah, the son of Amoz.

(2 Kings 19:3) 
And they delivered the message to him, saying: 'King Hezekiah says this: This is a day of tremendous anguish, of God's rebuke, and of blasphemy against the True God. It is as if a woman is in labor, and the children are ready to be born, but she has no strength left to deliver them. We are in a critical situation, but we are too weak to bring about our own rescue.'

(2 Kings 19:4) 
'Perhaps the Lord your God will hear all the words of Sennacherib, whom the king of Assyria, his master, has sent to insult the living God, and will rebuke him for the words which the Lord your God has heard. Therefore, lift up your prayers for the small remnant that remains of us.'

(2 Kings 19:5) 
So the servants of King Hezekiah came to Isaiah.

(2 Kings 19:6) 
And Isaiah said to them: 'Say this to your master: The Lord says this: Do not be afraid because of the words you have heard, with which the servants of the king of Assyria have blasphemed Me.'

(2 Kings 19:7) 
'Behold, I will put a spirit within him (a divine impulse of fear or decision), and he will hear a rumor and return to his own land. And there, in his own land, I will cause him to be struck down by the sword.'

(2 Kings 19:8) 
So Rabshakeh returned and found the king of Assyria warring against Libnah, because he had moved his forces from Lachish.

(2 Kings 19:9) 
For Sennacherib had heard a report about Tirhakah, the king of Ethiopia, that he had set out to fight against him. And so, the king of Assyria again sent messengers to King Hezekiah, saying:

(2 Kings 19:10) 
'Say this to Hezekiah, king of Judah: Do not let your God, in whom you trust, deceive you by saying: Jerusalem will not be given into the hand of the king of Assyria.'

(2 Kings 19:11) 
'Behold, you have heard what the kings of Assyria have done to all the other lands, how they utterly destroyed them. And do you think you can be delivered?'

(2 Kings 19:12) 
'Did the gods of the nations deliver those whom my fathers destroyed, like Gozan, and Haran, and Rezeph, and the people of Eden who were in Telassar?'

(2 Kings 19:13) 
'Where is the king of Hamath, and the king of Arpad, and the king of the city of Sepharvaim, of Hena, and of Ivvah?'

(2 Kings 19:14) 
And Hezekiah received the letters (the written messages) from the hand of the messengers and read them. Then he went up to the Temple of the Lord and spread the letters out before the Lord.

(2 Kings 19:15) 
And Hezekiah prayed in His presence, saying: 'O Lord, God of Israel, Who sits enthroned above the Cherubim (representing Your divine presence), You alone are God of all the kingdoms of the earth; You created the entire universe and the earth.'

(2 Kings 19:16) 
'Incline Your ear, O Lord, and hear! Open Your eyes, O Lord, and see! And listen to all the words of Sennacherib, who has sent his messengers to insult the living God.'

(2 Kings 19:17) 
'It is true, O Lord, that the kings of Assyria have devastated the nations and their lands, destroying all of them.'

(2 Kings 19:18) 
'And they threw the pagan gods of those nations into the fire, for those were not truly gods, but merely the work of human hands, made of wood and stone. That is why the Assyrians were able to destroy them.'

(2 Kings 19:19) 
'Now therefore, O Lord our God, save us from his hand, so that all the kingdoms of the earth may know that You alone are the Lord God.'

(2 Kings 19:20) 
Then Isaiah, the son of Amoz, sent a message to Hezekiah, saying: 'Thus says the Lord God of Israel: I have heard your prayer regarding Sennacherib, the king of Assyria.'

(2 Kings 19:21) 
'This is the word which the Lord has spoken concerning him: The virgin daughter of Zion (Jerusalem) despises you and mocks you; the daughter of Jerusalem shakes her head behind you (in derision).'

(2 Kings 19:22) 
'Whom have you insulted and blasphemed? Against whom have you raised your voice and lifted your eyes so proudly to the heavens? It was against the Holy One of Israel!'

(2 Kings 19:23) 
'By the hand of your messengers, you have insulted the Lord, and you said: “With the multitude of my chariots, I have ascended the highest mountains, to the peaks of Lebanon. I have cut down its tallest cedars and its choicest fir trees. I penetrated its farthest reaches, even to its thickest forest, like the forest of Carmel.”'

(2 Kings 19:24) 
'“I myself have dug wells and drunk foreign waters. With the sole of my foot, I have dried up all the rivers of the besieged lands.”'

(2 Kings 19:25) 
'Have you not heard that I (God) have prepared this from long ago? From ancient days, I (God) planned it, and now I (God) have brought it to pass. It was I who decreed that fortified cities would become heaps of rubble.'

(2 Kings 19:26) 
'And those who lived in those cities were put to shame and trembled with fear. They became like the grass of the field, and like the tender herb of the pasture, and like the dry grass on the rooftops, which withers before it grows tall.'

(2 Kings 19:27) 
'I (God) know your dwelling, and your going out, and your coming in, and your way of life. All your movements and intentions were known to Me.'

(2 Kings 19:28) 
'Because you have raged against Me, and your arrogance has reached My ears, I will put My hook in your nose and My bridle in your lips, and I will lead you back by the very way you came.'

(2 Kings 19:29) 
'And this will be a sign to you, Hezekiah: This year, you will eat what grows of itself (from the scattered seeds of the previous harvest); and in the second year, you will eat what springs up from that. But in the third year, you will sow and reap, and plant vineyards and eat their fruit.'

(2 Kings 19:30) 
'And the remnant that escapes from the house of Judah and remains will again take root downward and bear fruit upward.'

(2 Kings 19:31) 
'For from Jerusalem will go forth a remnant, and from Mount Zion will come those who are saved. The zeal of the Lord of hosts will accomplish this.'

(2 Kings 19:32) 
'Therefore, thus says the Lord concerning the king of Assyria: He will not enter this city, nor will he shoot an arrow into it, nor will he bring a shield against it, nor will he build up a siege ramp against it.'

(2 Kings 19:33) 
'By the way he came, by the same way he will return. And this city he will not enter, declares the Lord.'

(2 Kings 19:34) 
'For I will protect this city and save it, for My own sake and for the sake of My servant David.'

(2 Kings 19:35) 
And it came to pass that very night, the Angel of the Lord went out and struck down one hundred and eighty-five thousand men in the camp of the Assyrians. When people rose early in the morning, they saw all the bodies of the dead.

(2 Kings 19:36) 
So Sennacherib, king of Assyria, broke camp and departed. He returned to his land and remained in Nineveh.

(2 Kings 19:37) 
And as he was worshipping in the temple of Nisroch, his pagan god, Adrammelech and Sharezer, his sons, struck him down with the sword. They then fled into the land of Armenia. And Esarhaddon, his son, reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 19:1) 
When King Hezekiah heard this message of blasphemy and threat, he tore his royal garments in a display of profound grief and lamentation. He then wrapped himself in sackcloth, as a visible sign of his humility and penitence before God. After this, he immediately went into the Temple of the Lord to seek divine counsel.

(2 Kings 19:2) 
King Hezekiah sent Eliakim, who was the royal steward in charge of the palace, along with Shebna the royal secretary, and the most senior priests—all of whom were also dressed in sackcloth as a sign of their shared national distress—to the prophet Isaiah, the son of Amoz, to seek a prophetic word from God.

(2 Kings 19:3) 
These men then reported to Isaiah King Hezekiah's words: 'This day is a day of deep affliction, of severe rebuke from God, and of direct blasphemy against His holy Name, hurled by the Assyrians. Our nation is in such a desperate state, like a woman in labor who has reached the point of birth but lacks the strength to deliver her child—we are at the brink of destruction with no power to save ourselves.'

(2 Kings 19:4) 
'Perhaps the Lord your God has heard all the words of Rabshakeh, whom the King of Assyria, his master, sent to mock and insult the living God, and the Lord your God will condemn these words that He heard: please, then, raise up a fervent prayer for the small remnant of our people who are still left and found alive.'

(2 Kings 19:5) 
So, the servants of King Hezekiah—Eliakim, Shebna, and the senior priests—came before the prophet Isaiah.

(2 Kings 19:6) 
And Isaiah declared to them, 'Tell your master, King Hezekiah, this message from the Lord: 'Do not be afraid because of the words you have heard—the blasphemous insults uttered against Me by the servants of the King of Assyria. These are mere human words, but they are an affront to the living God.''

(2 Kings 19:7) 
'Indeed, I am now going to put a spirit into him—a spirit of fear and urgency—so that when he hears a report about his homeland, he will immediately return to his own country. And there, in his own land, I will cause him to fall by the sword; he will meet a violent end.'

(2 Kings 19:8) 
So Rabshakeh, the Assyrian general, returned to his king, Sennacherib, and found him battling near Libnah. This was because Rabshakeh had already heard that the Assyrian king had withdrawn from his siege of Lachish.

(2 Kings 19:9) 
Now, King Sennacherib had received a report concerning Tirhakah, the King of Ethiopia (also known as the Kushite King), stating: 'Indeed, he has marched out to wage war against you.' Because of this new threat, Sennacherib once again sent messengers to King Hezekiah of Judah, instructing them with a new message, saying:

(2 Kings 19:10) 
'Say this to King Hezekiah of Judah: 'Do not let your God, in whom you place your trust and confidence, deceive you by saying: 'Jerusalem will not be handed over into the power of the King of Assyria.' Do not be misled by such false hope.''

(2 Kings 19:11) 
'Look, you have heard and know very well what the kings of Assyria have done to all the other countries they have conquered, and how utterly and powerfully they have devastated them and brought them to ruin. So, do you truly believe that you alone will be able to be delivered from our grasp?'

(2 Kings 19:12) 
'Have the false gods of the nations—each one of them—been able to deliver their people whom my forefathers destroyed? Have they saved Gozan, Haran, Rezeph, or the people of Eden who lived in Telassar? No, they have not!'

(2 Kings 19:13) 
'Where are the kings of Hamath and Arpad now? And where is the king of the city of Sepharvaim? And where are the kings of Hena and Ivvah? They are all gone, conquered by my armies, and their gods could not save them!'

(2 Kings 19:14) 
King Hezekiah received these threatening letters from the messengers and read their blasphemous contents. He then went up into the Temple of the Lord, and there, in a powerful act of supplication and faith, Hezekiah spread out the letters before the Lord God.

(2 Kings 19:15) 
And Hezekiah prayed fervently in the sight of the Lord, declaring: 'O Lord, the God of Israel, You who are enthroned above the cherubim—You alone are the one true God of all the kingdoms and nations of the earth. You are the Almighty Creator, for You made the entire universe and the earth!'

(2 Kings 19:16) 
'Incline Your ear, O Lord, and hear our plea! Open Your divine eyes, O Lord, and see our distress! Hear all the words of Sennacherib, which he sent to mock and insult You, the living God, and thereby bring shame upon us, Your people.'

(2 Kings 19:17) 
'It is true, O Lord, that the kings of Assyria have indeed laid waste to the nations and all their lands, just as their arrogant letter boasts.'

(2 Kings 19:18) 
'And they have thrown the false gods of those nations into the fire and destroyed them. But these so-called gods were not truly gods at all; they were merely the lifeless work of human hands, fashioned from wood and stone, and thus the Assyrians were able to easily destroy them.'

(2 Kings 19:19) 
'Now therefore, O Lord our God, we humbly pray: deliver us from the powerful hand of this Assyrian king, Sennacherib. Do this so that all the kingdoms and nations of the earth may come to know and acknowledge that You alone are the Lord God, the one true and living God!'

(2 Kings 19:20) 
Then Isaiah, the son of Amoz, sent a message back to King Hezekiah, saying: 'This is what the Lord, the God of Israel, declares: 'I have heard your earnest prayer concerning Sennacherib, the King of Assyria. Your petition has reached My ears.''

(2 Kings 19:21) 
'This is the divine word which the Lord has spoken concerning Sennacherib: 'The virgin daughter of Zion—meaning the city of Jerusalem, pristine and undefiled by conquest—despises you and scoffs at your threats! The daughter of Jerusalem shakes her head in defiant scorn at you, King Sennacherib!''

(2 Kings 19:22) 
'Whom have you mocked and insulted with your words? Against whom have you raised your voice in arrogance, and lifted your eyes in proud defiance? You have done this against the Holy One of Israel Himself!'

(2 Kings 19:23) 
'By the hands of your messengers, you have indeed mocked the Lord God, and you have arrogantly declared: 'With my vast chariots and my mighty army, I have ascended the highest mountain peaks, even the very summits of Lebanon! I have cut down its tallest cedars and its finest cypress trees, and I have penetrated deep into its farthest reaches, into its most fertile forest, like its glorious Carmel!''

(2 Kings 19:24) 
'You have further boasted: 'I have personally dug wells and drunk foreign waters in conquered lands, and with the sole of my foot, I have dried up all the fortified streams of Egypt!' Your arrogance knows no bounds, thinking your power is limitless.

(2 Kings 19:25) 
'Have you not heard, King Sennacherib, that I, the Lord, have ordained this from long ago? From ancient times, I planned it, shaping and forming it according to My divine will. And now, I have brought it to pass: that you would turn fortified cities into piles of rubble, becoming a ruin of warring hills and strong cities, all as part of My grand design!'

(2 Kings 19:26) 
'Therefore, the inhabitants of those conquered lands had little power to resist; they were utterly dismayed and disgraced. They became like the grass of the field and the tender green plants, easily withered; like the thin grass on rooftops, scorched and dried up before it grows tall.'

(2 Kings 19:27) 
'But I, the Lord, know your every move: your comings and goings, your habitation, your entire way of life, and your journeying—I know all of it! And I also know the raging madness and fury that you direct against Me, the living God!'

(2 Kings 19:28) 
'Because you have raged against Me, and your arrogant boasting has ascended into My ears, I will place a hook in your nose and a bridle in your lips, just as one would do to a wild, untamed beast. And I will turn you back and lead you away along the very road by which you came, forcing your retreat!'

(2 Kings 19:29) 
'And this, O King Hezekiah, will be a miraculous sign for you and your people that My promise is true: This year, you will eat what grows naturally from scattered seeds left over from the previous harvest. And in the second year, you will eat what springs up from the ground on its own, without cultivation. But in the third year, you will once again be able to sow seed and reap a proper harvest, and you will plant vineyards and eat their fruits in peace.'

(2 Kings 19:30) 
'And the remnant that has escaped from the House of Judah—those who remain faithful and alive—will again take deep root downward into the land, and they will produce abundant fruit upward, signifying spiritual and physical prosperity.'

(2 Kings 19:31) 
'For from Jerusalem will come forth a saving remnant, and those who remain alive from Mount Zion—they will be preserved. The fervent zeal and passionate determination of the Lord of Hosts, the Commander of heavenly armies, will accomplish this divine intervention!'

(2 Kings 19:32) 
Therefore, this is what the Lord declares concerning the King of Assyria: 'He shall not enter this holy city of Jerusalem; he will not even shoot an arrow into it. No shield will be raised against it, nor will he build a siege ramp against its walls, for I will protect it absolutely.'

(2 Kings 19:33) 
'By the very road he came, by that same road will he return in retreat. He shall not enter this city,' declares the Lord, the Almighty God.

(2 Kings 19:34) 
'For I will protect this city, and I will save it, not for their merit, but for My own honor and glory, and for the sake of My loyal servant, King David, and the covenant I made with him.'

(2 Kings 19:35) 
So, it came to pass on that very night, that an angel of the Lord—a powerful heavenly messenger—went out and struck down one hundred and eighty-five thousand men in the Assyrian military camp. When the remaining Assyrians rose early the next morning, they looked out and saw all these vast numbers of dead bodies, realizing a catastrophic supernatural event had occurred.

(2 Kings 19:36) 
Then King Sennacherib of Assyria broke camp and departed immediately. He retreated from the land of Judah, returned to his own territory, and settled in his capital city, Nineveh.

(2 Kings 19:37) 
And it happened that while Sennacherib was worshipping in the temple of Nisroch, his demon-god, his own sons, Adrammelech and Sharezer, struck him down with a sword and killed him. They then fled for their lives to the land of Armenia. And Esarhaddon, another of Sennacherib's sons, became king in his place.

(2 Kings 20:1) 
In those days, King Hezekiah became so sick he was going to die. The prophet Isaiah, son of Amoz, went in to see him and said, "This is what the Lord says: Set your affairs in order, because you will die and not live."

(2 Kings 20:2) 
Hezekiah turned his face towards the wall and prayed to the Lord, saying:

(2 Kings 20:3) 
"I beg you, Lord, please remember, I pray, how I have walked before you in truth and with a perfect heart, and how I have done what was pleasing in your sight." And Hezekiah wept with great sorrow.

(2 Kings 20:4) 
And it happened, before Isaiah had even left the middle part of the courtyard, that the word of the Lord came to him, saying:

(2 Kings 20:5) 
Go back, and tell Hezekiah, the leader of my people: "This is what the Lord, the God of your father David, says: I have heard your prayer and I have seen your tears. Indeed, I will add fifteen years to your life."

(2 Kings 20:6) 
And I will deliver you and this city from the hand of the king of the Assyrians, and I will protect this city.

(2 Kings 20:7) 
Then Isaiah said, "Bring a poultice of figs." When they brought it and applied it to his boil, he recovered.

(2 Kings 20:8) 
Now Hezekiah had said to Isaiah, "What will be the sign that the Lord will heal me and that I will go up to the Temple of the Lord on the third day?"

(2 Kings 20:9) 
Isaiah answered him, "This will be the sign from the Lord, that the Lord will do what He has promised: Do you want the shadow to advance ten steps, or to go back ten steps?"

(2 Kings 20:10) 
Hezekiah said, "It is easy for the shadow to move forward ten steps, but I do not want that. Instead, let the shadow move back ten steps."

(2 Kings 20:11) 
Then the prophet Isaiah called upon the Lord, and God made the shadow on the sundial of Ahaz go back ten steps, which it had already descended.

(2 Kings 20:12) 
At that time, Berodach-Baladan, son of Baladan, the king of the Babylonians, sent letters and gifts to King Hezekiah, for he had heard that Hezekiah had been sick.

(2 Kings 20:13) 
Hezekiah heard what they had to say, and then he showed them all the treasures of his palace: the silver, the gold, the spices, the precious oils, his armory, and everything that could be found in his treasuries. There was nothing in his palace or in all his dominion that Hezekiah did not show them.

(2 Kings 20:14) 
Then the prophet Isaiah came to King Hezekiah and asked him, "What did these men say, and where did they come from?" Hezekiah replied, "They came from a distant land, from Babylon."

(2 Kings 20:15) 
Isaiah then asked, "What did they see in your palace?" Hezekiah answered, "They saw everything that is in my palace. There was nothing in my treasuries that I did not show them."

(2 Kings 20:16) 
Then Isaiah said to Hezekiah, "Hear the word of the Lord."

(2 Kings 20:17) 
Behold, the days are coming when everything in your palace, and everything that your ancestors have stored up until this day, will be carried off to Babylon. Nothing will be left, says the Lord.

(2 Kings 20:18) 
And some of your sons, who will come from you, whom you will father, will be taken and made eunuchs in the palace of the king of Babylon.

(2 Kings 20:19) 
Hezekiah said to Isaiah, "The word of the Lord that you have spoken is good. Let there be peace and stability during my lifetime."

(2 Kings 20:20) 
As for the rest of the acts of Hezekiah, and all his might, and how he made the pool and the aqueduct and brought water into the city—are these not written in the book of the chronicles of the kings of Judah?

(2 Kings 20:21) 
And Hezekiah slept with his ancestors, and his son Manasseh reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 21:1) 
Manasseh was twelve years old when he began to reign. He reigned for fifty-five years in Jerusalem. The name of his mother was Hephzibah.

(2 Kings 21:2) 
He did what was evil in the sight of the Lord, following the abominable practices of the pagan nations that the Lord had driven out from before the children of Israel.

(2 Kings 21:3) 
For he rebuilt the high places that his father Hezekiah had destroyed. He set up altars for Baal and made sacred groves, just as Ahab, the king of Israel, had done. Manasseh also worshipped all the celestial bodies of the sky, serving them as gods.

(2 Kings 21:4) 
He built altars in the Temple of the Lord, regarding which the Lord had said: “In Jerusalem I will establish my presence.”

(2 Kings 21:5) 
He built altars for all the celestial bodies of the sky in both courtyards of the Temple of the Lord.

(2 Kings 21:6) 
He also made his son pass through fire as a sacrifice, consulted diviners, practiced sorcery, engaged in witchcraft, and greatly multiplied those who consulted familiar spirits and wizards. He did much evil in the sight of the Lord, provoking Him to anger.

(2 Kings 21:7) 
He placed an idol of the sacred grove, which he had made, in the Temple of the Lord. Regarding this Temple, the Lord had said to David and to his son Solomon: “In this Temple and in Jerusalem, which I have chosen from all the tribes of Israel, I will establish my name forever.”

(2 Kings 21:8) 
And I will no longer allow the feet of Israel to wander from the land which I gave to their fathers—that is, if they will carefully observe my commandments and the entire Law that I commanded them through my servant Moses.

(2 Kings 21:9) 
But they did not listen. Instead, Manasseh misled them to do more evil than the pagan nations whom the Lord had destroyed before the children of Israel.

(2 Kings 21:10) 
Then the Lord spoke through His servants, the prophets, saying:

(2 Kings 21:11) 
“Because Manasseh, the king of Judah, has committed these most wicked abominations—doing more evil than all the Amorites before him—and has also made Judah sin with his idols,

(2 Kings 21:12) 
therefore, this is what the Lord God of Israel says: 'Behold, I am bringing such disaster upon Jerusalem and Judah that the ears of everyone who hears about it will ring with terror.

(2 Kings 21:13) 
I will stretch over Jerusalem the measuring line of Samaria and the plumb line of the house of Ahab. I will wipe away Jerusalem as one wipes a dish, wiping it and turning it upside down.

(2 Kings 21:14) 
And I will abandon the remnant of my heritage, delivering them into the hand of their enemies. They will become plunder and a prey to all their adversaries,

(2 Kings 21:15) 
because they have done evil in my sight and have provoked me to anger, from the day their fathers came out of Egypt until this very day.’”

(2 Kings 21:16) 
Moreover, Manasseh shed so much innocent blood that he filled Jerusalem from one end to the other. This was in addition to his sins by which he made Judah sin and do evil in the sight of the Lord.

(2 Kings 21:17) 
Now the rest of the acts of Manasseh, and all that he did, and the sin he committed, are these not written in the Book of the Annals of the Kings of Judah?

(2 Kings 21:18) 
Manasseh rested with his fathers and was buried in the garden of his own house, in the Garden of Uzza. And his son Amon reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 21:19) 
Amon was twenty-two years old when he began to reign. He reigned for two years in Jerusalem. The name of his mother was Meshullemeth, the daughter of Haruz, from Jotbah.

(2 Kings 21:20) 
He did what was evil in the sight of the Lord, just as his father Manasseh had done.

(2 Kings 21:21) 
He walked in every way that his father had walked. He served the detestable idols that his father had served and worshipped them.

(2 Kings 21:22) 
He abandoned the Lord, the God of his fathers, and did not walk in the way of the Lord.

(2 Kings 21:23) 
Now the servants of Amon conspired against him and killed the king in his own house.

(2 Kings 21:24) 
But the people of the land struck down all those who had conspired against King Amon. Then they made his son Josiah king in his place.

(2 Kings 21:25) 
Now the rest of the acts of Amon which he did, are these not written in the Book of the Annals of the Kings of Judah?

(2 Kings 21:26) 
They buried him in his tomb in the Garden of Uzza. And his son Josiah reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 21:27) 
Josiah was eight years old when he began to reign. He reigned for thirty-one years in Jerusalem. The name of his mother was Jedidah, the daughter of Adaiah, from Bozkath.

(2 Kings 21:28) 
And he did what was right in the sight of the Lord. He walked in all the ways of his father David, not turning aside to the right or to the left.

(2 Kings 21:29) 
Now in the eighth year of his reign, while he was still a boy, he began to seek the God of his father David. In the twelfth year of his reign, he began to cleanse Judah and Jerusalem of the high places, the sacred groves, the carved idols, and the molten images.

(2 Kings 21:30) 
Before him, they tore down the altars of the Baals, and he smashed the altars that were placed above them. He cut down the sacred groves and the carved idols, breaking them into pieces. He pulverized the idol fields and scattered their dust over the tombs of those who had offered sacrifices there.

(2 Kings 21:31) 
He also burned the altars of the idols all around, cut down the sacred groves, and crushed the carved and molten images into dust. He scattered the dust over the graves of the common people.

(2 Kings 21:32) 
He did likewise in the cities of Manasseh, Ephraim, and Simeon, and as far as Naphtali, destroying idolatry throughout the surrounding areas.

(2 Kings 21:33) 
When he returned to Jerusalem, passing through the Temple of the Lord, he found the book of the Law of the Lord given through Moses in the Temple of the Lord.

(2 Kings 21:34) 
This is what Hilkiah the priest found and gave to Shaphan the scribe, and he read it in the presence of the king.

(2 Kings 21:35) 
When the king heard the words of the Law of the Lord, he tore his garments and prayed to the Lord.

(2 Kings 21:36) 
Then he commanded Hilkiah the priest, and Ahikam, the son of Shaphan, and Abdon, the son of Micah, and Shaphan the scribe, and Asaiah, the king’s servant, saying:

(2 Kings 21:37) 
“Go, inquire of the Lord for me, and for the people, and for all Judah, concerning the words of this book that has been found. For great is the wrath of the Lord that has been poured out upon us, because our fathers have not kept the words of the Lord to do everything written in this book.”

(2 Kings 21:38) 
So Hilkiah the priest, and Ahikam, and Abdon, and Shaphan, and Asaiah, went to Huldah the prophetess, the wife of Shallum, the son of Tikvah, the son of Harhas, the keeper of the wardrobe. She lived in Jerusalem in the Second Quarter. They spoke to her the words that had been previously said to them.

(2 Kings 21:39) 
And she answered them: “Thus says the Lord God of Israel: ‘Tell the man who sent you to me:

(2 Kings 21:40) 
“This is what the Lord says: 'Behold, I will bring disaster upon this place and upon its inhabitants, all the curses that are written in this book which they have read before the king of Judah.

(2 Kings 22:1) 
Josias was eight years old when he became king, and he reigned for thirty-one years in Jerusalem. His mother's name was Idida, daughter of Adaja, from the town of Besecath.

(2 Kings 22:2) 
He did what was right in the eyes of the Lord. He walked in the full path of his father David, not turning aside to the right or to the left.

(2 Kings 22:3) 
In the eighteenth year of King Josias's reign, the king sent Saphan, son of Asaliah, son of Messulam, who was the scribe of the Lord's Temple, saying:

(2 Kings 22:4) 
"Go to Helcias, the high priest. Tell him to assess the money that has been brought into the Lord's Temple. This money was collected by the doorkeepers from the people."

(2 Kings 22:5) 
"And let him give this money to the carpenters, and to the masons, and to those who cut stones. Let them also buy wood and cut stones so that they may repair and rebuild the Temple of the Lord."

(2 Kings 22:6) 
There was no accounting required with them for the money they received, because they acted with faithfulness and integrity.

(2 Kings 22:7) 
Then Helcias the high priest said to Saphan the scribe, "I have found the Book of the Law in the house of the Lord." And Helcias gave the book to Saphan, who then read it.

(2 Kings 22:8) 
Saphan the scribe came to the king and reported what the king had commanded him. Afterwards, Saphan informed the king, saying, "Helcias the priest gave me a book." When Saphan read the book aloud before the king,

(2 Kings 22:9) 
and the king heard the words of the book, which was the Law of the Lord, he tore his clothes in grief and repentance.

(2 Kings 22:10) 
And he commanded Helcias the priest, and Achicam the son of Saphan, and Achobor the son of Micha, and Saphan the scribe, and Asaiah the king's servant, saying:

(2 Kings 22:11) 
"Go and consult the Lord on my behalf, and on behalf of the people, and on behalf of all Judah, concerning the words of this book that has been found. For the great wrath of the Lord has been kindled against us, because our fathers did not listen to the words of this book, so as to do all that is written and commanded for us."

(2 Kings 22:12) 
So Helcias the priest, and Achicam, and Achobor, and Saphan, and Asaiah, went to Olda the prophetess. She was the wife of Sellum, son of Thecue, son of Araas, the keeper of the vestments. She was living in Jerusalem in the Second Quarter. They spoke to her, seeking guidance.

(2 Kings 22:13) 
And she answered them, saying, "Thus says the Lord God of Israel: 'Tell the man who sent you to me:

(2 Kings 22:14) 
Thus says the Lord: Behold, I will bring evil upon this place and upon its inhabitants—all the words of the book which the king of Judah has read. This punishment will certainly come to pass."

(2 Kings 22:15) 
"This is because they have abandoned Me and have sacrificed to foreign gods, provoking Me to anger with all the works of their hands. Therefore, My indignation has been kindled against this place, and it will not be extinguished."

(2 Kings 22:16) 
"But to the king of Judah, who sent you to consult the Lord, thus you shall say: 'Thus says the Lord God of Israel: Because you have heard the words of the scroll,

(2 Kings 22:17) 
and your heart was humbled, and you humbled yourself before the Lord when you heard the words against this place and against its inhabitants—that they would become a desolation and a curse—and you tore your garments and wept before Me, I have heard you, says the Lord."

(2 Kings 22:18) 
"Therefore, behold, I will gather you to your fathers, and you shall be gathered to your tomb in peace. Your eyes shall not see all the evils which I am about to bring upon this place.'" And they reported to the king everything she had said.

(2 Kings 22:19) 
So the king sent messengers and gathered to himself all the elders of Judah and Jerusalem.

(2 Kings 22:20) 
And the king went up to the Temple of the Lord, and all the men of Judah, and all those who lived in Jerusalem went with him—the priests and the prophets, and all the people, from the least important to the greatest. And he read in their hearing all the words of the book of the covenant, which had been found in the house of the Lord.

(2 Kings 22:1) 
Josiah was eight years old when he began to reign. He reigned for thirty-one years in Jerusalem. His mother's name was Idida, daughter of Adaja, from Besecath.

(2 Kings 22:2) 
He did what was right in the sight of the Lord, and he walked in all the ways of his father David. He did not turn aside to the right hand or to the left.

(2 Kings 22:3) 
In the eighteenth year of King Josiah's reign, the king sent Shaphan, the son of Azaliah, the son of Meshullam, who was the scribe of the Temple of the Lord. The king said to him:

(2 Kings 22:4) 
“Go to Hilkiah, the high priest, so that he may gather the money that has been brought into the Temple of the Lord by the doorkeepers, who were entrusted with keeping it safe.

(2 Kings 22:5) 
And let him give this money to the workmen, to the carpenters, and to the masons, who are repairing the Temple of the Lord.

(2 Kings 22:6) 
And let them use the money to restore the walls, and to buy timber and cut stones for the repair of the places in the Temple of the Lord.”

(2 Kings 22:7) 
However, the money that they receive for this work is not to be accounted for, but it is to be given to them based on their authority and trustworthiness.

(2 Kings 22:8) 
Then Hilkiah, the high priest, said to Shaphan, the scribe, “I have found the Book of the Law in the house of the Lord.” Hilkiah gave the book to Shaphan, and Shaphan read it.

(2 Kings 22:9) 
Shaphan, the scribe, came back to the king and reported what had been commanded. Furthermore, he informed the king, saying, “Your servants have given the money that was found in the house of the Lord to the workmen and to those who supervise the work in the Temple of the Lord.”

(2 Kings 22:10) 
Shaphan, the scribe, also told the king, saying, “Hilkiah, the priest, gave me a book.” And when Shaphan had read it in the presence of the king,

(2 Kings 22:11) 
and the king heard the words of the book of the Law of the Lord, he tore his garments in a sign of deep distress and repentance.

(2 Kings 22:12) 
Then the king commanded Hilkiah the priest, and Ahikam the son of Shaphan, and Achbor the son of Micaiah, and Shaphan the scribe, and Asaiah, the king's servant, saying:

(2 Kings 22:13) 
“Go, and consult the Lord on my behalf, and on behalf of the people, and on behalf of all Judah, concerning the words of this book that has been found. For the wrath of the Lord is greatly kindled against us, because our fathers did not listen to the words of this book, to do all that is written for us.”

(2 Kings 22:14) 
So Hilkiah the priest, and Ahikam, and Achbor, and Shaphan, and Asaiah went to Huldah the prophetess. She was the wife of Shallum, son of Tikvah, son of Harhas, who was the keeper of the wardrobe. She lived in Jerusalem in the Second Quarter of the city. They spoke to her.

(2 Kings 22:15) 
And she answered them, “Thus says the Lord, the God of Israel: ‘Tell the man who sent you to me:

(2 Kings 22:16) 
Thus says the Lord: Behold, I am bringing disaster upon this place and upon its inhabitants, according to all the words of the book that the king of Judah has read.

(2 Kings 22:17) 
Because they have abandoned Me and have offered sacrifices to other gods, provoking Me with all the works of their hands, My indignation will be kindled against this place, and it will not be extinguished.’

(2 Kings 22:18) 
But to the king of Judah, who sent you to consult the Lord, you shall say this: ‘Thus says the Lord, the God of Israel: Because you have heard the words of the book,

(2 Kings 22:19) 
and your heart was moved, and you humbled yourself before the Lord when you heard the words against this place and against its inhabitants, that they would become a desolation and a curse—and because you tore your garments and wept before Me—I also have heard it,’ says the Lord.

(2 Kings 22:20) 
Therefore, behold, I will gather you to your fathers, and you shall be gathered to your grave in peace. Your eyes will not see all the evil that I am bringing upon this place.” And they reported her words to the king.

(2 Kings 23:1) 
King Josiah then sent messengers, and all the elders of Judah and Jerusalem gathered before him.

(2 Kings 23:2) 
The king went up to the Temple of the Lord, and with him were all the men of Judah, everyone living in Jerusalem, the priests, the prophets, and all the people, from the youngest to the oldest. He read aloud to everyone the words of the book of the covenant that had been found in the house of the Lord.

(2 Kings 23:3) 
The king then stood by the pillar and made a covenant before the Lord, promising to follow the Lord, to keep His commandments, His testimonies, and His ceremonies with all his heart and all his soul, and to uphold the words of this covenant that were written in this book. And all the people agreed to the covenant.

(2 Kings 23:4) 
The king then commanded Hilkiah the high priest, the priests of the second rank, and the doorkeepers to remove from the Temple of the Lord all the vessels that had been made for Baal, for Asherah, and for all the host of the heavens—meaning, all the objects used for pagan worship. He burned these things outside Jerusalem in the Kidron Valley and carried their ashes to Bethel.

(2 Kings 23:5) 
He abolished the idolatrous priests whom the kings of Judah had appointed to burn incense on the high places throughout the cities of Judah and around Jerusalem. He also abolished those who burned incense to Baal, to the sun, to the moon, to the constellations, and to all the host of the heavens.

(2 Kings 23:6) 
He had the Asherah pole—a pagan idol—carried out from the Lord’s house, outside Jerusalem, into the Kidron Valley. He burned it there, ground it to dust, and threw the dust on the graves of the common people to defile them.

(2 Kings 23:7) 
He also tore down the quarters of the male cult prostitutes, which were in the house of the Lord, where women wove garments for the idol Asherah.

(2 Kings 23:8) 
He gathered all the priests from the cities of Judah and defiled the high places where these priests had offered sacrifices, from Geba to Beersheba. He also tore down the altars at the gates, specifically the one at the entrance of the gate of Joshua, the governor of the city, which was on the left side as one entered the gate.

(2 Kings 23:9) 
However, the priests from these high places were not allowed to ascend to the Lord’s altar in Jerusalem; instead, they ate unleavened bread among their fellow priests.

(2 Kings 23:10) 
He also defiled Topheth, which is in the Valley of Ben Hinnom, so that no one could consecrate his son or daughter by passing them through fire to Molech—a horrific pagan ritual.

(2 Kings 23:11) 
And he removed the horses that the kings of Judah had dedicated to the sun god, located at the entrance of the house of the Lord, near the chamber of Nathan-Melech, the eunuch who was in charge of the storerooms. He also burned the chariots of the sun with fire.

(2 Kings 23:12) 
The king also tore down the altars that were on the roof of Ahaz’s upper chamber, which the kings of Judah had made. And he destroyed the altars that Manasseh had made in the two courtyards of the house of the Lord. He ran from there, broke them into pieces, ground them to dust, and scattered the dust over the Kidron stream.

(2 Kings 23:13) 
The king also defiled the high places that were in Jerusalem, on the right side of the Mount of Corruption—meaning, the Mount of Olives. These had been built by King Solomon of Israel for Ashtoreth, the idol of the Sidonians; for Chemosh, the idol of the Moabites; and for Molech, the idol of the Ammonites.

(2 Kings 23:14) 
He smashed the pagan pillars, cut down the Asherah poles, and filled their places with the bones of the dead—an act of severe defilement.

(2 Kings 23:15) 
Moreover, he also tore down the altar in Bethel and the high place that Jeroboam son of Nebat had built, the one who caused Israel to sin. He overthrew that altar and that high place. He burned the high place, ground it to dust, and burned the Asherah pole.

(2 Kings 23:16) 
Then Josiah turned and saw the tombs that were on the mountain there. He sent men to take the bones from the tombs, and he burned them on the altar, thus defiling it, just as the word of the Lord had declared through the man of God who had prophesied these very things.

(2 Kings 23:17) 
He asked, 'What is that monument I see?' The men of the city answered him, 'It is the tomb of the man of God who came from Judah and foretold these very things that you have done against the altar of Bethel.'

(2 Kings 23:18) 
And he said, 'Leave him alone; let no one disturb his bones.' So his bones remained untouched, along with the bones of the prophet who had come from Samaria.

(2 Kings 23:19) 
Moreover, Josiah also removed all the shrines of the high places in the cities of Samaria, which the kings of Israel had made to provoke the Lord to anger. He dealt with them in the same way he had dealt with all the works he did in Bethel.

(2 Kings 23:20) 
He slaughtered all the priests of the high places who were there, sacrificing them on their own altars, and he burned human bones upon those altars to defile them thoroughly. Then he returned to Jerusalem.

(2 Kings 23:21) 
Then the king commanded all the people, saying, 'Celebrate the Passover to the Lord your God, according to what is written in this book of the covenant.'

(2 Kings 23:22) 
Indeed, no such Passover had been celebrated from the days of the judges who judged Israel, nor in all the days of the kings of Israel or the kings of Judah,

(2 Kings 23:23) 
as it was observed for the Lord in Jerusalem in the eighteenth year of King Josiah's reign.

(2 Kings 23:24) 
Furthermore, Josiah also removed the mediums, the spiritists, the household idols, the pagan images, and all the detestable things that had been in the land of Judah and Jerusalem. He did this to fulfill the words of the Law that were written in the book which Hilkiah the priest had found in the Temple of the Lord.

(2 Kings 23:25) 
Before him, there was no king like him, who turned back to the Lord with all his heart, with all his soul, and with all his strength, according to all the Law of Moses; nor did any like him arise after him.

(2 Kings 23:26) 
Nevertheless, the Lord did not turn away from the fierceness of His great wrath, with which He was angry against Judah because of all the provocations with which Manasseh had provoked Him.

(2 Kings 23:27) 
And the Lord said, 'I will also remove Judah from my sight, just as I removed Israel. I will cast away this city of Jerusalem, which I chose, and the house about which I said, 'My Name shall be there.''

(2 Kings 23:28) 
Now the rest of the acts of Josiah, and all that he did, are these not written in the book of the chronicles of the kings of Judah?

(2 Kings 23:29) 
In his days, Pharaoh Necho, king of Egypt, went up against the king of Assyria to the Euphrates River. King Josiah went out to meet him, and Pharaoh Necho killed him at Megiddo the moment he saw him.

(2 Kings 23:30) 
His servants carried his dead body from Megiddo and brought him to Jerusalem and buried him in his own tomb. Then the people of the land took Jehoahaz, Josiah's son, anointed him, and made him king in place of his father.

(2 Kings 23:31) 
Jehoahaz was twenty-three years old when he began to reign, and he reigned for three months in Jerusalem. His mother’s name was Hamutal, daughter of Jeremiah, from Libnah.

(2 Kings 23:32) 
He did what was evil in the sight of the Lord, just as all his fathers had done.

(2 Kings 23:33) 
Pharaoh Necho imprisoned him at Riblah in the land of Hamath, so that he could not reign in Jerusalem. And Pharaoh imposed a tax on the land: one hundred talents of silver and one talent of gold.

(2 Kings 23:34) 
Pharaoh Necho then made Eliakim, Josiah's son, king in place of Josiah his father, and changed his name to Jehoiakim. As for Jehoahaz, Pharaoh took him and brought him to Egypt, where he died.

(2 Kings 23:35) 
Jehoiakim gave the silver and gold to Pharaoh, after he had heavily taxed the land to pay the money according to Pharaoh's command. He exacted the silver and gold from each person according to their means, taking it from the people of the land to give to Pharaoh Necho.

(2 Kings 23:36) 
Jehoiakim was twenty-five years old when he began to reign, and he reigned for eleven years in Jerusalem. His mother’s name was Zebidah, daughter of Pedaiah, from Rumah.

(2 Kings 23:37) 
He did what was evil in the sight of the Lord, just as all his fathers had done.

(2 Kings 23:38) 
In Jehoiakim’s days, Nebuchadnezzar, king of Babylon, came up, and Jehoiakim became his servant for three years; then he turned and rebelled against him again.

(2 Kings 23:39) 
And the Lord sent against him raiding bands of Chaldeans, raiding bands of Syrians, raiding bands of Moabites, and raiding bands of the Ammonites. He sent them against Judah to destroy it, according to the word of the Lord which He had spoken through His servants, the prophets.

(2 Kings 23:40) 
Indeed, this came to pass in Judah according to the word of the Lord, so that He might remove them from His presence because of all the sins of Manasseh that he had committed.

(2 Kings 23:1) 
So the king sent out messengers and gathered to himself all the elders of Judah and Jerusalem.

(2 Kings 23:2) 
And the king went up to the Temple of the Lord. All the men of Judah, everyone found in Jerusalem, the priests, the prophets, and all the people—from the least to the greatest—went with him. Then the king read, while everyone listened, all the words of the covenant book that had been found in the Temple of the Lord.

(2 Kings 23:3) 
The king stood on his royal platform and made a solemn covenant before the Lord: that they would walk after the Lord and keep His commandments, His testimonies, and His statutes with all their heart and all their soul. They also promised to confirm the words of this covenant, which were written in this book. And all the people agreed to this covenant.

(2 Kings 23:4) 
Then the king commanded Hilkiah, the high priest, the priests of the second order, and the doorkeepers to remove from the Temple of the Lord all the vessels that had been made for Baal, for Asherah, and for all the host of the sky. He burned these items outside Jerusalem, in the Kidron Valley, and carried their ashes to Bethel.

(2 Kings 23:5) 
He removed the idolatrous priests whom the kings of Judah had appointed to burn incense at the high places throughout the cities of Judah and around Jerusalem. He also removed those who burned incense to Baal, to the sun, to the moon, to the twelve constellations of the zodiac, and to all the host of the sky.

(2 Kings 23:6) 
He had the Asherah poles brought out from the Temple of the Lord, outside Jerusalem into the Kidron Valley. There he burned them, ground them to powder, and scattered the powder over the graves of the common people.

(2 Kings 23:7) 
He also tore down the living quarters of the male prostitutes, those who served false gods, which were in the Temple of the Lord, where women wove garments, like small shelters for idols.

(2 Kings 23:8) 
He gathered all the priests from the cities of Judah. He desecrated the high places where the priests had offered sacrifices, from Geba to Beer-sheba. He also tore down the altars at the entrance of the gate of Joshua, the governor of the city, which was to the left of the city gate.

(2 Kings 23:9) 
However, the priests of the high places were not permitted to ascend to the altar of the Lord in Jerusalem. Instead, they ate unleavened bread among their fellow priests.

(2 Kings 23:10) 
He also desecrated Topheth, which is in the Valley of the Son of Hinnom, so that no one could consecrate his son or his daughter by passing them through fire to Moloch.

(2 Kings 23:11) 
He removed the horses that the kings of Judah had dedicated to the sun god, at the entrance to the Temple of the Lord, near the chamber of Nathan-melech, the eunuch who was in charge of the courtyards. And the chariots of the sun god he burned with fire.

(2 Kings 23:12) 
The altars that were on the roof of the upper chamber of Ahaz, which the kings of Judah had made, and the altars that Manasseh had made in the two courtyards of the Temple of the Lord—these the king destroyed. He quickly ran and broke them down from that place and brought their ashes to the Kidron torrent.

(2 Kings 23:13) 
The high places that were in Jerusalem, on the right side of the Mount of Offense, which Solomon, king of Israel, had built for Ashtoreth, the idol of the Sidonians, and for Chemosh, the idol of the Moabites, and for Milcom (Moloch), the idol of the Ammonites—these the king desecrated.

(2 Kings 23:14) 
He broke the sacred pillars into pieces and cut down the Asherah poles. Then he filled their places with the bones of the dead.

(2 Kings 23:15) 
Furthermore, the altar that was in Bethel, and the high place that Jeroboam, the son of Nebat, had made—he who caused Israel to sin—both that altar and that high place Josiah destroyed. He burned it, crushed it to powder, and burned the Asherah pole as well.

(2 Kings 23:16) 
Then Josiah turned and saw the tombs that were there on the mountain. He sent men, took the bones from the tombs, and burned them on the altar, thereby desecrating it, just as the word of the Lord had declared through the man of God who had proclaimed these words long ago.

(2 Kings 23:17) 
And he asked, 'What is that monument I see there?' The men of the city replied to him, 'It is the tomb of the man of God who came from Judah and announced these very things that you have done against the altar of Bethel.'

(2 Kings 23:18) 
Josiah said, 'Leave him alone. Let no one disturb his bones.' So his bones remained untouched, along with the bones of the prophet who had come from Samaria.

(2 Kings 23:19) 
Moreover, all the shrines of the high places that were in the cities of Samaria, which the kings of Israel had made to provoke the Lord to anger, Josiah removed. He did to them exactly as he had done in Bethel.

(2 Kings 23:20) 
He sacrificed all the priests of the high places who were there on the altars, and he burned human bones upon them. Then he returned to Jerusalem.

(2 Kings 23:21) 
The king commanded all the people, saying, 'Celebrate the Passover to the Lord your God, as it is written in this covenant book.'

(2 Kings 23:22) 
For such a Passover had not been celebrated since the days of the judges who judged Israel, nor in all the days of the kings of Israel or the kings of Judah.

(2 Kings 23:23) 
But in the eighteenth year of King Josiah's reign, this Passover was celebrated to the Lord in Jerusalem with great devotion.

(2 Kings 23:24) 
Furthermore, Josiah abolished the mediums and spiritists, the household gods and idols, and all the abominations that were found in the land of Judah and in Jerusalem. He did this to uphold the words of the law that were written in the book which Hilkiah the priest found in the Temple of the Lord.

(2 Kings 23:25) 
There was no king like him before him, who turned to the Lord with all his heart, with all his soul, and with all his might, according to all the Law of Moses. Nor did any like him arise after him.

(2 Kings 23:26) 
Nevertheless, the Lord did not turn away from the fierceness of His great wrath, with which His anger burned against Judah because of all the provocations by which Manasseh had provoked Him.

(2 Kings 23:27) 
And the Lord said, 'I will also remove Judah from my presence, just as I removed Israel. I will cast away this city of Jerusalem, which I chose, and the house about which I said, 'My Name shall be there.''

(2 Kings 23:28) 
Now the rest of the acts of Josiah, and all that he did, are they not written in the Book of the Chronicles of the Kings of Judah?

(2 Kings 23:29) 
In his days, Pharaoh Neco, king of Egypt, went up to attack the king of Assyria by the Euphrates River. King Josiah went out to meet him in battle, and when Josiah saw him, he was killed at Megiddo.

(2 Kings 23:30) 
His servants carried his dead body from Megiddo, brought him to Jerusalem, and buried him in his own tomb. Then the people of the land took Jehoahaz, Josiah's son, anointed him, and made him king in place of his father.

(2 Kings 23:31) 
Jehoahaz was twenty-three years old when he began to reign, and he reigned for three months in Jerusalem. His mother's name was Hamutal, daughter of Jeremiah, from Libnah.

(2 Kings 23:32) 
He did what was evil in the sight of the Lord, just as all his fathers had done.

(2 Kings 23:33) 
Pharaoh Neco imprisoned him at Riblah, in the land of Hamath, so that he could not reign in Jerusalem. He also imposed a fine on the land: one hundred talents of silver and one talent of gold.

(2 Kings 23:34) 
Pharaoh Neco made Eliakim, Josiah's son, king in place of Josiah his father, and changed his name to Jehoiakim. As for Jehoahaz, Pharaoh took him away to Egypt, and he died there.

(2 Kings 23:35) 
Jehoiakim gave the silver and gold to Pharaoh after he had levied the amount from each person according to their means. He exacted the silver and gold from the people of the land to give to Pharaoh Neco.

(2 Kings 23:36) 
Jehoiakim was twenty-five years old when he began to reign, and he reigned for eleven years in Jerusalem. His mother's name was Zebidah, daughter of Pedaiah, from Rumah.

(2 Kings 23:37) 
He did what was evil in the sight of the Lord, just as all his fathers had done.

(2 Kings 23:38) 
In his days, Nebuchadnezzar, king of Babylon, came up, and Jehoiakim became his servant for three years. Then he rebelled against him again.

(2 Kings 23:39) 
The Lord sent against him raiding bands of Chaldeans, bands of Syrians, bands of Moabites, and bands of the Ammonites' sons. He sent them against Judah to destroy it, according to the word of the Lord, which He had spoken through His servants the prophets.

(2 Kings 23:40) 
For indeed, this happened in Judah according to the word of the Lord, that He might remove them from His presence because of all the sins of Manasseh and all that he had done.

(2 Kings 23:1) 
So King Josiah sent out orders, and all the elders of Judah and Jerusalem were gathered to him.

(2 Kings 23:2) 
The king then went up to the Temple of the Lord, God’s holy dwelling, and with him were all the men of Judah, and everyone who was found in Jerusalem—the priests, the prophets, and all the people, from the youngest to the oldest. He read aloud for all to hear all the words of the book of the covenant, the rediscovered ancient law of God, which had been found in the Temple of the Lord.

(2 Kings 23:3) 
The king stood by the pillar and made a solemn covenant before the Lord, promising that he and his people would walk after the Lord, and would keep His commandments, and His testimonies, and His sacred rites with all their heart and with all their soul. They vowed to confirm all the words of this covenant which were written in this book. And all the people confirmed their agreement to the covenant.

(2 Kings 23:4) 
The king then commanded Hilkiah the high priest, the priests of the second rank who assisted him, and the Temple doorkeepers, to remove from the Temple of the Lord all the items that had been made for the worship of Baal, and for the goddess Asherah, and for the worship of all the celestial bodies—the host of heaven. Josiah had these items burned outside Jerusalem in the Kidron Valley, a place of defilement, and had their ashes carried to Bethel, a site historically associated with Israelite idolatry.

(2 Kings 23:5) 
He then removed the pagan priests whom the kings of Judah had appointed to burn incense on the high places, in the cities of Judah, and in the areas around Jerusalem. These priests had burned incense to Baal, to the sun, to the moon, to the twelve constellations of the zodiac, and to all the celestial bodies—the host of heaven.

(2 Kings 23:6) 
He brought the Asherah pole, an idolatrous cult object, out from the Temple of the Lord to the Kidron Valley in Jerusalem. There he burned it, ground it into dust, and scattered the dust over the graves of the common people, further defiling it.

(2 Kings 23:7) 
He also tore down the living quarters of the male cult prostitutes, which were located within the Temple of the Lord, where women wove hangings for the Asherah.

(2 Kings 23:8) 
Josiah then brought all the priests from the cities of Judah to Jerusalem. He defiled the high places where these priests had offered sacrifices, from Geba in the north all the way to Beersheba in the south. He also demolished the altars at the entrance of the gate of Joshua, the governor of the city, which was to the left of the city gate.

(2 Kings 23:9) 
However, those priests who had served at the high places were not permitted to ascend to the altar of the Lord in Jerusalem; instead, they were only allowed to eat unleavened bread among their fellow priests, but not participate in the sacred Temple rituals.

(2 Kings 23:10) 
He also defiled Topheth, which is in the Valley of the Son of Hinnom, to prevent anyone from consecrating their son or daughter by passing them through fire as a sacrifice to the pagan god Moloch. This practice of child sacrifice was abolished.

(2 Kings 23:11) 
He removed the horses that the kings of Judah had dedicated to the sun god, which were at the entrance of the Temple of the Lord, near the chamber of Nathan-Melech, the eunuch who was in charge of the sacred courtyard. He also burned the chariots of the sun with fire.

(2 Kings 23:12) 
The altars that were on the roof of the upper chamber of Ahaz, which the kings of Judah had built, and the altars that Manasseh had built in the two courtyards of the Temple of the Lord, these the king tore down. He quickly broke them into pieces and scattered their dust into the Kidron Valley.

(2 Kings 23:13) 
King Josiah also defiled the high places that were opposite Jerusalem, to the right of the Mount of Offence—also known as the Mount of Olives. These had been built by Solomon, the king of Israel, for Ashtoreth, the detestable idol of the Sidonians; for Chemosh, the detestable idol of Moab; and for Milcom, the detestable idol of the Ammonites. Josiah rendered these sites unusable for worship.

(2 Kings 23:14) 
He smashed the sacred pillars, cut down the Asherah poles, and filled their places with the bones of the dead, further desecrating these pagan sites.

(2 Kings 23:15) 
Furthermore, Josiah destroyed the altar at Bethel and the high place that Jeroboam son of Nebat had made, the very one who led Israel into sin. He pulled down that altar and the high place, burned them, crushed them to dust, and also burned the Asherah pole.

(2 Kings 23:16) 
As Josiah turned around, he saw the tombs that were on the mountain nearby. He sent and took the bones from those tombs, then burned them on the altar, thus defiling it. This action fulfilled the word of the Lord that had been spoken by the man of God, the prophet who had foretold these very events.

(2 Kings 23:17) 
And Josiah asked: 'What is that monument I see there?' The people of the city answered him: 'It is the tomb of the man of God, a prophet who came from Judah and foretold these very things that you have done against the altar of Bethel.'

(2 Kings 23:18) 
So Josiah said: 'Leave him be; let no one disturb his bones.' Thus, his bones were left undisturbed, along with the bones of the prophet who had come from Samaria.

(2 Kings 23:19) 
Josiah also removed all the shrines of the high places in the cities of Samaria, which the kings of Israel had built to provoke the Lord to anger. He dealt with them in the same manner as all the things he had done in Bethel, thoroughly destroying them.

(2 Kings 23:20) 
He sacrificed all the priests of the high places who were there, offering them on their own altars. He also burned human bones on those altars, further desecrating them. After these purifications, Josiah returned to Jerusalem.

(2 Kings 23:21) 
Then King Josiah commanded all the people, saying: 'Celebrate the Passover to the Lord your God, exactly as it is written in this book of the covenant.'

(2 Kings 23:22) 
Indeed, such a Passover had not been celebrated from the days when the judges ruled Israel, nor during all the days of the kings of Israel and the kings of Judah.

(2 Kings 23:23) 
It was in the eighteenth year of King Josiah’s reign that this Passover was celebrated for the Lord in Jerusalem, a celebration of unparalleled devotion.

(2 Kings 23:24) 
Moreover, Josiah removed all the mediums who consulted the dead, the spiritists, the household idols known as teraphim, the detestable false gods, and all the other abominations that had been present in the land of Judah and in Jerusalem. He did this to establish the words of the Law that were written in the book which Hilkiah the priest had found in the Temple of the Lord.

(2 Kings 23:25) 
No king before him had turned to the Lord with all his heart, with all his soul, and with all his strength, according to all the Law of Moses. Nor did any like him arise after him. Josiah’s devotion was truly exceptional.

(2 Kings 23:26) 
Nevertheless, the Lord did not turn away from the great fierceness of His burning anger, with which His wrath had been kindled against Judah because of all the provocations that Manasseh, a former king, had caused.

(2 Kings 23:27) 
So the Lord said: 'I will also remove Judah from My presence, just as I removed Israel, the northern kingdom. I will cast away this city of Jerusalem, which I chose, and the Temple, about which I said: My Name shall be there.' This signified a complete rejection due to the people's persistent sin.

(2 Kings 23:28) 
Now the rest of the acts of Josiah, and all that he did, are these not written in the book of the chronicles of the kings of Judah?

(2 Kings 23:29) 
In those days, Pharaoh Necho, the king of Egypt, went up to attack the king of Assyria by the Euphrates River. King Josiah went out to confront him, and was killed at Megiddo as soon as he saw him in battle.

(2 Kings 23:30) 
His servants carried his dead body from Megiddo and brought him to Jerusalem, where they buried him in his own tomb. Then the people of the land took Jehoahaz, Josiah’s son, anointed him, and made him king in place of his father.

(2 Kings 23:31) 
Jehoahaz was twenty-three years old when he began to reign, and he reigned for three months in Jerusalem. His mother's name was Hamutal, the daughter of Jeremiah, from Libnah.

(2 Kings 23:32) 
He did evil in the sight of the Lord, just as all his predecessors, his fathers, had done.

(2 Kings 23:33) 
Then Pharaoh Necho imprisoned him at Riblah, which is in the land of Hamath, to prevent him from reigning in Jerusalem. The Pharaoh also imposed a tribute on the land: one hundred talents of silver and one talent of gold.

(2 Kings 23:34) 
Pharaoh Necho then made Eliakim, Josiah’s son, king in place of his father Josiah, and changed Eliakim’s name to Jehoiakim. As for Jehoahaz, Necho took him away to Egypt, and he died there.

(2 Kings 23:35) 
Jehoiakim paid Pharaoh the silver and gold he demanded, collecting it by imposing a tax on each person according to their means. He exacted the money from the people of the land and gave it to Pharaoh Necho.

(2 Kings 23:36) 
Jehoiakim was twenty-five years old when he began to reign, and he reigned for eleven years in Jerusalem. His mother’s name was Zebidah, the daughter of Pedaiah, from Rumah.

(2 Kings 23:37) 
He did evil in the sight of the Lord, just as all his predecessors, his fathers, had done.

(2 Kings 23:38) 
In his days, Nebuchadnezzar, king of Babylon, came up against Jerusalem. Jehoiakim became his servant for three years, but then he rebelled against him.

(2 Kings 23:39) 
And the Lord sent against him raiding parties of the Chaldeans, and raiding parties of the Syrians, and raiding parties of the Moabites, and raiding parties of the Ammonites. He sent them against Judah to destroy it, according to the word of the Lord, which He had spoken through His servants the prophets.

(2 Kings 23:40) 
Indeed, this came upon Judah by the anger of the Lord, so that He might remove them from His presence because of the sins of Manasseh, according to all that he had done.

(2 Kings 24:1) 
In those days, King Nebuchadnezzar of Babylon marched on Judah, and Jehoiakim, king of Judah, became his vassal for three years, but then Jehoiakim rebelled against him.

(2 Kings 24:2) 
And the Lord God sent against Jehoiakim raiding parties of Chaldeans, and raiding parties of Syrians, and raiding parties of Moabites, and raiding parties of the Ammonites. He sent them into Judah to destroy it, just as the Lord God had declared through His servants, the prophets.

(2 Kings 24:3) 
This happened in Judah precisely as the Lord God had declared: He removed them from His sight because of all the sins of Manasseh, everything that Manasseh had done,

(2 Kings 24:4) 
and because of the innocent blood that Manasseh had shed, filling Jerusalem with innocent blood. Because of this, the Lord God refused to forgive them.

(2 Kings 24:5) 
All the other deeds of Jehoiakim, and everything he did, are they not written in the Book of the Annals of the Kings of Judah?

(2 Kings 24:6) 
Jehoiakim died and was buried with his ancestors. Then his son Jehoiachin reigned in his place.

(2 Kings 24:7) 
And the king of Egypt never again left his own territory, because the king of Babylon had taken all the lands that once belonged to the king of Egypt, from the Brook of Egypt all the way to the Euphrates River.

(2 Kings 24:8) 
Jehoiachin was eighteen years old when he became king, and he reigned in Jerusalem for three months. His mother's name was Nehushta, daughter of Elnathan, from Jerusalem.

(2 Kings 24:9) 
And Jehoiachin did evil in the sight of the Lord God, just as his father had done.

(2 Kings 24:10) 
At that time, the army of the king of Babylon marched up to Jerusalem, and the city was put under siege.

(2 Kings 24:11) 
King Nebuchadnezzar of Babylon himself came to the city while his servants were besieging it.

(2 Kings 24:12) 
So Jehoiachin, the king of Judah, surrendered to the king of Babylon—he, his mother, his servants, his princes, and his eunuchs all surrendered. The king of Babylon accepted his surrender in the eighth year of his own reign.

(2 Kings 24:13) 
Nebuchadnezzar carried away all the treasures from the House of the Lord God and all the treasures from the king's palace. He also cut into pieces all the golden vessels that King Solomon of Israel had made for the Temple of the Lord God, just as the Lord God had declared.

(2 Kings 24:14)
