He deported all of Jerusalem's inhabitants, all the leaders, and all the mighty warriors—ten thousand of them into captivity. He also took every craftsman and every blacksmith, leaving behind only the poorest people of the land.

(2 Kings 24:15) 
Nebuchadnezzar also deported Jehoiachin to Babylon, along with the king's mother, the king's wives, his eunuchs, and all the powerful men of the land were taken captive from Jerusalem to Babylon.

(2 Kings 24:16) 
And all the strong men, numbering seven thousand, and a thousand craftsmen and blacksmiths—all of them strong and capable warriors—the king of Babylon brought them as captives to Babylon.

(2 Kings 24:17) 
And the king of Babylon then made Mattaniah, Jehoiachin's uncle, king in his place, changing his name to Zedekiah.

(2 Kings 24:18) 
Zedekiah was twenty-one years old when he became king, and he reigned in Jerusalem for eleven years. His mother's name was Hamutal, daughter of Jeremiah, from Libnah.

(2 Kings 24:19) 
And Zedekiah did evil in the sight of the Lord God, just as Jehoiakim had done.

(2 Kings 24:20) 
For the Lord God was so angry with Jerusalem and with Judah that He eventually cast them out from His sight. Zedekiah also rebelled against the king of Babylon.

(2 Kings 25:1) 
It happened in the ninth year of King Sedecias’ reign, on the tenth day of the tenth month. Nebuchadnezzar, the king of Babylon, came to Jerusalem with his entire army. They surrounded the city and built siege works all around it.

(2 Kings 25:2) 
The city remained under siege until the eleventh year of King Sedecias’ reign.

(2 Kings 25:3) 
On the ninth day of that month, a severe famine gripped the city, and the people of the land had no bread left to eat.

(2 Kings 25:4) 
The city’s defenses were breached, and all the fighting men fled by night through the gate between the two walls that led to the king’s garden. The Chaldeans were surrounding the city at this point. The king himself escaped by way of the plain.

(2 Kings 25:5) 
The Chaldean army pursued the king and captured him in the desert plains near Jericho. All the soldiers who had been with him were scattered away from him.

(2 Kings 25:6) 
So, the captured king was brought to the king of Babylon in Riblah, which is in the land of Hamath. There, Nebuchadnezzar pronounced judgment against him.

(2 Kings 25:7) 
Nebuchadnezzar then killed Sedecias’ sons right before his eyes. After that, he gouged out Sedecias’ eyes, bound him with bronze chains, and took him to Babylon.

(2 Kings 25:8) 
In the fifth month, on the seventh day of the month—which was the nineteenth year of the reign of Nebuchadnezzar, the king of Babylon—Nabuzardan, the commander of the royal guard and a servant of the king of Babylon, came to Jerusalem.

(2 Kings 25:9) 
He set fire to the Temple of the Lord, the king’s palace, and all the houses of Jerusalem. Every important building was burned down.

(2 Kings 25:10) 
The entire Chaldean army, who were with the commander of the royal guard, tore down the walls of Jerusalem all around the city.

(2 Kings 25:11) 
Nabuzardan, the commander of the royal guard, carried away into exile the rest of the people who had remained in the city, along with those who had deserted to the king of Babylon, and all the other common people.

(2 Kings 25:12) 
However, the commander of the royal guard left behind some of the poorest people of the land to be vine dressers and farmers.

(2 Kings 25:13) 
The Chaldeans also broke into pieces the bronze pillars that were in the Temple of the Lord, as well as the movable stands and the great bronze basin known as the Sea, which was also in the Lord's Temple. They carried all the bronze from these items away to Babylon.

(2 Kings 25:14) 
They also took away the bronze pots, shovels, snuffers, ladles, and all the other bronze vessels used in the Temple service.

(2 Kings 25:15) 
The commander of the royal guard also took away the fire pans and the sprinkling bowls, distinguishing those made of solid gold and those made of solid silver.

(2 Kings 25:16) 
As for the two pillars, the one great Sea, and the movable stands that Solomon had made for the Temple of the Lord, the amount of bronze in all these vessels was immeasurable.

(2 Kings 25:17) 
Each pillar was eighteen cubits high, with a bronze capital three cubits high on top. Over the capital, there was bronze lattice work and pomegranates, all made of bronze. The second pillar was identical to this one.

(2 Kings 25:18) 
The commander of the royal guard also took away Saraias, the chief priest, Sophonias, the second priest, and the three doorkeepers.

(2 Kings 25:19) 
From the city, he took one eunuch who was in charge of the fighting men; five men who served as the king's close advisors, whom he found in the city; Sopher, the commander of the army; and sixty men from the common people of the land, who were also found in the city.

(2 Kings 25:20) 
Nabuzardan, the commander of the royal guard, took all these men and brought them to the king of Babylon in Riblah.

(2 Kings 25:21) 
There, in Riblah, in the land of Hamath, the king of Babylon struck them down and put them to death. So, Judah was carried away from its own land into exile.

(2 Kings 25:22) 
As for the people who had been left behind in the land of Judah, those whom Nebuchadnezzar, the king of Babylon, had permitted to remain, he appointed Godolias, the son of Ahicam, the son of Saphan, over them.

(2 Kings 25:23) 
When all the army commanders—they and their men—heard that the king of Babylon had appointed Godolias as governor, they came to Godolias in Maspha. These were Ismael, son of Nathanias; Joanan, son of Caree; Sarea, son of Thanehumeth the Netophathite; and Jezonias, son of Maachati, along with their companions.

(2 Kings 25:24) 
Godolias swore an oath to them and their companions, saying: 'Do not be afraid of serving the Chaldeans. Stay in the land and serve the king of Babylon, and it will go well for you.'

(2 Kings 25:25) 
However, in the seventh month, Ismael, the son of Nathanias, the son of Elisama, who was of royal lineage, came with ten men. They struck down Godolias, who died, and also killed the Jews and Chaldeans who were with him in Maspha.

(2 Kings 25:26) 
Then all the people, from the least to the greatest, along with the army commanders, were afraid. So they rose up and went to Egypt, fearing the Chaldeans’ retaliation.

(2 Kings 25:27) 
It happened in the thirty-seventh year of the exile of Joachin, the king of Judah, in the twelfth month, on the twenty-seventh day of the month. Evilmerodach, the king of Babylon, in the first year of his reign, released Joachin, the king of Judah, from prison.

(2 Kings 25:28) 
Evilmerodach spoke kindly to him and set his throne above the thrones of the other kings who were with him in Babylon.

(2 Kings 25:29) 
Joachin was allowed to change out of his prison clothes, and he was given food to eat continually in the king’s presence for the rest of his life.

(2 Kings 25:30) 
The king also provided him with a regular daily allowance of food for the remaining days of his life, until his death.
